Why, Lord love the\r\nman, whatever is the matter with him?\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94for as he had spoken the last\r\nfew words of his story Ernest had turned white as a sheet, and was\r\nleaning against the railings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJohn,\xe2\x80\x9d said my hero, gasping for breath, \xe2\x80\x9care you sure of what you\r\nsay\xe2\x80\x94are you quite sure you really married her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I am,\xe2\x80\x9d said John, \xe2\x80\x9cI married her before the registrar at\r\nLetchbury on the 15th of August 1851.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive me your arm,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ernest, \xe2\x80\x9cand take me into Piccadilly, and put\r\nme into a cab, and come with me at once, if you can spare time, to Mr\r\nOverton\xe2\x80\x99s at the Temple.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER LXXVII\r\n\r\n\r\nI do not think Ernest himself was much more pleased at finding that he\r\nhad never been married than I was. To him, however, the shock of\r\npleasure was positively numbing in its intensity. As he felt his burden\r\nremoved, he reeled for the unaccustomed lightness of his movements; his\r\nposition was so shattered that his identity seemed to have been\r\nshattered also; he was as one waking up from a horrible nightmare to\r\nfind himself safe and sound in bed, but who can hardly even yet believe\r\nthat the room is not full of armed men who are about to spring upon\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd it is I,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cwho not an hour ago complained that I was\r\nwithout hope. It is I, who for weeks have been railing at fortune, and\r\nsaying that though she smiled on others she never smiled at me. Why,\r\nnever was anyone half so fortunate as I am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, \xe2\x80\x9cyou have been inoculated for marriage, and have\r\nrecovered.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI was very fond of her till she took to drinking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps; but is it not Tennyson who has said: \xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x99Tis better to have\r\nloved and lost, than never to have lost at all\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are an inveterate bachelor,\xe2\x80\x9d was the rejoinder.\r\n\r\nThen we had a long talk with John, to whom I gave a \xc2\xa35 note upon the\r\nspot. He said, \xe2\x80\x9cEllen had used to drink at Battersby; the cook had\r\ntaught her; he had known it, but was so fond of her, that he had\r\nchanced it and married her to save her from the streets and in the hope\r\nof being able to keep her straight. She had done with him just as she\r\nhad done with Ernest\xe2\x80\x94made him an excellent wife as long as she kept\r\nsober, but a very bad one afterwards.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere isn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said John, \xe2\x80\x9ca sweeter-tempered, handier, prettier girl\r\nthan she was in all England, nor one as knows better what a man likes,\r\nand how to make him happy, if you can keep her from drink; but you\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t keep her; she\xe2\x80\x99s that artful she\xe2\x80\x99ll get it under your very eyes,\r\nwithout you knowing it. If she can\xe2\x80\x99t get any more of your things to\r\npawn or sell, she\xe2\x80\x99ll steal her neighbours\xe2\x80\x99. That\xe2\x80\x99s how she got into\r\ntrouble first when I was with her. During the six months she was in\r\nprison I should have felt happy if I had not known she would come out\r\nagain. And then she did come out, and before she had been free a\r\nfortnight, she began shop-lifting and going on the loose again\xe2\x80\x94and all\r\nto get money to drink with. So seeing I could do nothing with her and\r\nthat she was just a-killing of me, I left her, and came up to London,\r\nand went into service again, and I did not know what had become of her\r\ntill you and Mr Ernest here told me. I hope you\xe2\x80\x99ll neither of you say\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve seen me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe assured him we would keep his counsel, and then he left us, with\r\nmany protestations of affection towards Ernest, to whom he had been\r\nalways much attached.\r\n\r\nWe talked the situation over, and decided first to get the children\r\naway, and then to come to terms with Ellen concerning their future\r\ncustody; as for herself, I proposed that we should make her an\r\nallowance of, say, a pound a week to be paid so long as she gave no\r\ntrouble. Ernest did not see where the pound a week was to come from, so\r\nI eased his mind by saying I would pay it myself. Before the day was\r\ntwo hours older we had got the children, about whom Ellen had always\r\nappeared to be indifferent, and had confided them to the care of my\r\nlaundress, a good motherly sort of woman, who took to them and to whom\r\nthey took at once.\r\n\r\nThen came the odious task of getting rid of their unhappy mother.\r\nErnest\xe2\x80\x99s heart smote him at the notion of the shock the break-up would\r\nbe to her. He was always thinking that people had a claim upon him for\r\nsome inestimable service they had rendered him, or for some irreparable\r\nmischief done to them by himself; the case however was so clear, that\r\nErnest\xe2\x80\x99s scruples did not offer serious resistance.\r\n\r\nI did not see why he should have the pain of another interview with his\r\nwife, so I got Mr Ottery to manage the whole business. It turned out\r\nthat we need not have harrowed ourselves so much about the agony of\r\nmind which Ellen would suffer on becoming an outcast again. Ernest saw\r\nMrs Richards, the neighbour who had called him down on the night when\r\nhe had first discovered his wife\xe2\x80\x99s drunkenness, and got from her some\r\ndetails of Ellen\xe2\x80\x99s opinions upon the matter. She did not seem in the\r\nleast conscience-stricken; she said: \xe2\x80\x9cThank goodness, at last!\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\nalthough aware that her marriage was not a valid one, evidently\r\nregarded this as a mere detail which it would not be worth anybody\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhile to go into more particularly. As regards his breaking with her,\r\nshe said it was a good job both for him and for her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis life,\xe2\x80\x9d she continued, \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t suit me. Ernest is too good for me;\r\nhe wants a woman as shall be a bit better than me, and I want a man\r\nthat shall be a bit worse than him. We should have got on all very well\r\nif we had not lived together as married folks, but I\xe2\x80\x99ve been used to\r\nhave a little place of my own, however small, for a many years, and I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t want Ernest, or any other man, always hanging about it. Besides\r\nhe is too steady: his being in prison hasn\xe2\x80\x99t done him a bit of\r\ngood\xe2\x80\x94he\xe2\x80\x99s just as grave as those as have never been in prison at all,\r\nand he never swears nor curses, come what may; it makes me afeared of\r\nhim, and therefore I drink the worse. What us poor girls wants is not\r\nto be jumped up all of a sudden and made honest women of; this is too\r\nmuch for us and throws us off our perch; what we wants is a regular\r\nfriend or two, who\xe2\x80\x99ll just keep us from starving, and force us to be\r\ngood for a bit together now and again. That\xe2\x80\x99s about as much as we can\r\nstand. He may have the children; he can do better for them than I can;\r\nand as for his money, he may give it or keep it as he likes, he\xe2\x80\x99s never\r\ndone me any harm, and I shall let him alone; but if he means me to have\r\nit, I suppose I\xe2\x80\x99d better have it.\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94And have it she did.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Ernest to himself again when the arrangement was\r\nconcluded, \xe2\x80\x9cam the man who thought himself unlucky!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI may as well say here all that need be said further about Ellen. For\r\nthe next three years she used to call regularly at Mr Ottery\xe2\x80\x99s every\r\nMonday morning for her pound. She was always neatly dressed, and looked\r\nso quiet and pretty that no one would have suspected her antecedents.\r\nAt first she wanted sometimes to anticipate, but after three or four\r\nineffectual attempts\xe2\x80\x94on each of which occasions she told a most pitiful\r\nstory\xe2\x80\x94she gave it up and took her money regularly without a word. Once\r\nshe came with a bad black eye, \xe2\x80\x9cwhich a boy had throwed a stone and hit\r\nher by mistake\xe2\x80\x9d; but on the whole she looked pretty much the same at\r\nthe end of the three years as she had done at the beginning. Then she\r\nexplained that she was going to be married again. Mr Ottery saw her on\r\nthis, and pointed out to her that she would very likely be again\r\ncommitting bigamy by doing so. \xe2\x80\x9cYou may call it what you like,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nreplied, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I am going off to America with Bill the butcher\xe2\x80\x99s man,\r\nand we hope Mr Pontifex won\xe2\x80\x99t be too hard on us and stop the\r\nallowance.\xe2\x80\x9d Ernest was little likely to do this, so the pair went in\r\npeace. I believe it was Bill who had blacked her eye, and she liked him\r\nall the better for it.\r\n\r\nFrom one or two little things I have been able to gather that the\r\ncouple got on very well together, and that in Bill she has found a\r\npartner better suited to her than either John or Ernest. On his\r\nbirthday Ernest generally receives an envelope with an American\r\npost-mark containing a book-marker with a flaunting text upon it, or a\r\nmoral kettle-holder, or some other similar small token of recognition,\r\nbut no letter. Of the children she has taken no notice.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER LXXVIII\r\n\r\n\r\nErnest was now well turned twenty-six years old, and in little more\r\nthan another year and a half would come into possession of his money. I\r\nsaw no reason for letting him have it earlier than the date fixed by\r\nMiss Pontifex herself; at the same time I did not like his continuing\r\nthe shop at Blackfriars after the present crisis. It was not till now\r\nthat I fully understood how much he had suffered, nor how nearly his\r\nsupposed wife\xe2\x80\x99s habits had brought him to actual want.\r\n\r\nI had indeed noted the old wan worn look settling upon his face, but\r\nwas either too indolent or too hopeless of being able to sustain a\r\nprotracted and successful warfare with Ellen to extend the sympathy and\r\nmake the inquiries which I suppose I ought to have made. And yet I\r\nhardly know what I could have done, for nothing short of his finding\r\nout what he had found out would have detached him from his wife, and\r\nnothing could do him much good as long as he continued to live with\r\nher.\r\n\r\nAfter all I suppose I was right; I suppose things did turn out all the\r\nbetter in the end for having been left to settle themselves\xe2\x80\x94at any rate\r\nwhether they did or did not, the whole thing was in too great a muddle\r\nfor me to venture to tackle it so long as Ellen was upon the scene;\r\nnow, however, that she was removed, all my interest in my godson\r\nrevived, and I turned over many times in my mind, what I had better do\r\nwith him.\r\n\r\nIt was now three and a half years since he had come up to London and\r\nbegun to live, so to speak, upon his own account. Of these years, six\r\nmonths had been spent as a clergyman, six months in gaol, and for two\r\nand a half years he had been acquiring twofold experience in the ways\r\nof business and of marriage. He had failed, I may say, in everything\r\nthat he had undertaken, even as a prisoner; yet his defeats had been\r\nalways, as it seemed to me, something so like victories, that I was\r\nsatisfied of his being worth all the pains I could bestow upon him; my\r\nonly fear was lest I should meddle with him when it might be better for\r\nhim to be let alone. On the whole I concluded that a three and a half\r\nyears\xe2\x80\x99 apprenticeship to a rough life was enough; the shop had done\r\nmuch for him; it had kept him going after a fashion, when he was in\r\ngreat need; it had thrown him upon his own resources, and taught him to\r\nsee profitable openings all around him, where a few months before he\r\nwould have seen nothing but insuperable difficulties; it had enlarged\r\nhis sympathies by making him understand the lower classes, and not\r\nconfining his view of life to that taken by gentlemen only. When he\r\nwent about the streets and saw the books outside the second-hand\r\nbook-stalls, the bric-a-brac in the curiosity shops, and the infinite\r\ncommercial activity which is omnipresent around us, he understood it\r\nand sympathised with it as he could never have done if he had not kept\r\na shop himself.\r\n\r\nHe has often told me that when he used to travel on a railway that\r\noverlooked populous suburbs, and looked down upon street after street\r\nof dingy houses, he used to wonder what kind of people lived in them,\r\nwhat they did and felt, and how far it was like what he did and felt\r\nhimself. Now, he said he knew all about it. I am not very familiar with\r\nthe writer of the Odyssey (who, by the way, I suspect strongly of\r\nhaving been a clergyman), but he assuredly hit the right nail on the\r\nhead when he epitomised his typical wise man as knowing \xe2\x80\x9cthe ways and\r\nfarings of many men.\xe2\x80\x9d What culture is comparable to this? What a lie,\r\nwhat a sickly debilitating debauch did not Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s school and\r\nuniversity career now seem to him, in comparison with his life in\r\nprison and as a tailor in Blackfriars. I have heard him say he would\r\nhave gone through all he had suffered if it were only for the deeper\r\ninsight it gave him into the spirit of the Grecian and the Surrey\r\npantomimes. What confidence again in his own power to swim if thrown\r\ninto deep waters had not he won through his experiences during the last\r\nthree years!\r\n\r\nBut, as I have said, I thought my godson had now seen as much of the\r\nunder currents of life as was likely to be of use to him, and that it\r\nwas time he began to live in a style more suitable to his prospects.\r\nHis aunt had wished him to kiss the soil, and he had kissed it with a\r\nvengeance; but I did not like the notion of his coming suddenly from\r\nthe position of a small shop-keeper to that of a man with an income of\r\nbetween three and four thousand a year. Too sudden a jump from bad\r\nfortune to good is just as dangerous as one from good to bad; besides,\r\npoverty is very wearing; it is a quasi-embryonic condition, through\r\nwhich a man had better pass if he is to hold his later developments\r\nsecurely, but like measles or scarlet fever he had better have it\r\nmildly and get it over early.\r\n\r\nNo man is safe from losing every penny he has in the world, unless he\r\nhas had his facer. How often do I not hear middle-aged women and quiet\r\nfamily men say that they have no speculative tendency; _they_ never had\r\ntouched, and never would touch, any but the very soundest, best reputed\r\ninvestments, and as for unlimited liability, oh dear! dear! and they\r\nthrow up their hands and eyes.\r\n\r\nWhenever a person is heard to talk thus he may be recognised as the\r\neasy prey of the first adventurer who comes across him; he will\r\ncommonly, indeed, wind up his discourse by saying that in spite of all\r\nhis natural caution, and his well knowing how foolish speculation is,\r\nyet there are some investments which are called speculative but in\r\nreality are not so, and he will pull out of his pocket the prospectus\r\nof a Cornish gold mine. It is only on having actually lost money that\r\none realises what an awful thing the loss of it is, and finds out how\r\neasily it is lost by those who venture out of the middle of the most\r\nbeaten path. Ernest had had his facer, as he had had his attack of\r\npoverty, young, and sufficiently badly for a sensible man to be little\r\nlikely to forget it. I can fancy few pieces of good fortune greater\r\nthan this as happening to any man, provided, of course, that he is not\r\ndamaged irretrievably.\r\n\r\nSo strongly do I feel on this subject that if I had my way I would have\r\na speculation master attached to every school. The boys would be\r\nencouraged to read the _Money Market Review_, the _Railway News_, and\r\nall the best financial papers, and should establish a stock exchange\r\namongst themselves in which pence should stand as pounds. Then let them\r\nsee how this making haste to get rich moneys out in actual practice.\r\nThere might be a prize awarded by the head-master to the most prudent\r\ndealer, and the boys who lost their money time after time should be\r\ndismissed. Of course if any boy proved to have a genius for speculation\r\nand made money\xe2\x80\x94well and good, let him speculate by all means.\r\n\r\nIf Universities were not the worst teachers in the world I should like\r\nto see professorships of speculation established at Oxford and\r\nCambridge. When I reflect, however, that the only things worth doing\r\nwhich Oxford and Cambridge can do well are cooking, cricket, rowing and\r\ngames, of which there is no professorship, I fear that the\r\nestablishment of a professorial chair would end in teaching young men\r\nneither how to speculate, nor how not to speculate, but would simply\r\nturn them out as bad speculators.\r\n\r\nI heard of one case in which a father actually carried my idea into\r\npractice. He wanted his son to learn how little confidence was to be\r\nplaced in glowing prospectuses and flaming articles, and found him five\r\nhundred pounds which he was to invest according to his lights. The\r\nfather expected he would lose the money; but it did not turn out so in\r\npractice, for the boy took so much pains and played so cautiously that\r\nthe money kept growing and growing till the father took it away again,\r\nincrement and all\xe2\x80\x94as he was pleased to say, in self defence.\r\n\r\nI had made my own mistakes with money about the year 1846, when\r\neveryone else was making them. For a few years I had been so scared and\r\nhad suffered so severely, that when (owing to the good advice of the\r\nbroker who had advised my father and grandfather before me) I came out\r\nin the end a winner and not a loser, I played no more pranks, but kept\r\nhenceforward as nearly in the middle of the middle rut as I could. I\r\ntried in fact to keep my money rather than to make more of it. I had\r\ndone with Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s money as with my own\xe2\x80\x94that is to say I had let it\r\nalone after investing it in Midland ordinary stock according to Miss\r\nPontifex\xe2\x80\x99s instructions. No amount of trouble would have been likely to\r\nhave increased my godson\xe2\x80\x99s estate one half so much as it had increased\r\nwithout my taking any trouble at all.\r\n\r\nMidland stock at the end of August 1850, when I sold out Miss\r\nPontifex\xe2\x80\x99s debentures, stood at \xc2\xa332 per \xc2\xa3100. I invested the whole of\r\nErnest\xe2\x80\x99s \xc2\xa315,000 at this price, and did not change the investment till\r\na few months before the time of which I have been writing lately\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nis to say until September 1861. I then sold at \xc2\xa3129 per share and\r\ninvested in London and North-Western ordinary stock, which I was\r\nadvised was more likely to rise than Midlands now were. I bought the\r\nLondon and North-Western stock at \xc2\xa393 per \xc2\xa3100, and my godson now in\r\n1882 still holds it.\r\n\r\nThe original \xc2\xa315,000 had increased in eleven years to over \xc2\xa360,000; the\r\naccumulated interest, which, of course, I had re-invested, had come to\r\nabout \xc2\xa310,000 more, so that Ernest was then worth over \xc2\xa370,000. At\r\npresent he is worth nearly double that sum, and all as the result of\r\nleaving well alone.\r\n\r\nLarge as his property now was, it ought to be increased still further\r\nduring the year and a half that remained of his minority, so that on\r\ncoming of age he ought to have an income of at least \xc2\xa33500 a year.\r\n\r\nI wished him to understand book-keeping by double entry. I had myself\r\nas a young man been compelled to master this not very difficult art;\r\nhaving acquired it, I have become enamoured of it, and consider it the\r\nmost necessary branch of any young man\xe2\x80\x99s education after reading and\r\nwriting. I was determined, therefore, that Ernest should master it, and\r\nproposed that he should become my steward, book-keeper, and the manager\r\nof my hoardings, for so I called the sum which my ledger showed to have\r\naccumulated from \xc2\xa315,000 to \xc2\xa370,000. I told him I was going to begin to\r\nspend the income as soon as it had amounted up to \xc2\xa380,000.\r\n\r\nA few days after Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s discovery that he was still a bachelor, while\r\nhe was still at the very beginning of the honeymoon, as it were, of his\r\nrenewed unmarried life, I broached my scheme, desired him to give up\r\nhis shop, and offered him \xc2\xa3300 a year for managing (so far indeed as it\r\nrequired any managing) his own property. This \xc2\xa3300 a year, I need\r\nhardly say, I made him charge to the estate.\r\n\r\nIf anything had been wanting to complete his happiness it was this.\r\nHere, within three or four days he found himself freed from one of the\r\nmost hideous, hopeless _liaisons_ imaginable, and at the same time\r\nraised from a life of almost squalor to the enjoyment of what would to\r\nhim be a handsome income.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA pound a week,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought, \xe2\x80\x9cfor Ellen, and the rest for myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, \xe2\x80\x9cwe will charge Ellen\xe2\x80\x99s pound a week to the estate also.\r\nYou must have a clear \xc2\xa3300 for yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI fixed upon this sum, because it was the one which Mr Disraeli gave\r\nConingsby when Coningsby was at the lowest ebb of his fortunes. Mr\r\nDisraeli evidently thought \xc2\xa3300 a year the smallest sum on which\r\nConingsby could be expected to live, and make the two ends meet; with\r\nthis, however, he thought his hero could manage to get along for a year\r\nor two. In 1862, of which I am now writing, prices had risen, though\r\nnot so much as they have since done; on the other hand Ernest had had\r\nless expensive antecedents than Coningsby, so on the whole I thought\r\n\xc2\xa3300 a year would be about the right thing for him.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER LXXIX\r\n\r\n\r\nThe question now arose what was to be done with the children. I\r\nexplained to Ernest that their expenses must be charged to the estate,\r\nand showed him how small a hole all the various items I proposed to\r\ncharge would make in the income at my disposal. He was beginning to\r\nmake difficulties, when I quieted him by pointing out that the money\r\nhad all come to me from his aunt, over his own head, and reminded him\r\nthere had been an understanding between her and me that I should do\r\nmuch as I was doing, if occasion should arise.\r\n\r\nHe wanted his children to be brought up in the fresh pure air, and\r\namong other children who were happy and contented; but being still\r\nignorant of the fortune that awaited him, he insisted that they should\r\npass their earlier years among the poor rather than the rich. I\r\nremonstrated, but he was very decided about it; and when I reflected\r\nthat they were illegitimate, I was not sure but that what Ernest\r\nproposed might be as well for everyone in the end. They were still so\r\nyoung that it did not much matter where they were, so long as they were\r\nwith kindly decent people, and in a healthy neighbourhood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be just as unkind to my children,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cas my grandfather\r\nwas to my father, or my father to me. If they did not succeed in making\r\ntheir children love them, neither shall I. I say to myself that I\r\nshould like to do so, but so did they. I can make sure that they shall\r\nnot know how much they would have hated me if they had had much to do\r\nwith me, but this is all I can do. If I must ruin their prospects, let\r\nme do so at a reasonable time before they are old enough to feel it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe mused a little and added with a laugh:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA man first quarrels with his father about three-quarters of a year\r\nbefore he is born. It is then he insists on setting up a separate\r\nestablishment; when this has been once agreed to, the more complete the\r\nseparation for ever after the better for both.\xe2\x80\x9d Then he said more\r\nseriously: \xe2\x80\x9cI want to put the children where they will be well and\r\nhappy, and where they will not be betrayed into the misery of false\r\nexpectations.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the end he remembered that on his Sunday walks he had more than once\r\nseen a couple who lived on the waterside a few miles below Gravesend,\r\njust where the sea was beginning, and who he thought would do. They had\r\na family of their own fast coming on and the children seemed to thrive;\r\nboth father and mother indeed were comfortable well grown folks, in\r\nwhose hands young people would be likely to have as fair a chance of\r\ncoming to a good development as in those of any whom he knew.\r\n\r\nWe went down to see this couple, and as I thought no less well of them\r\nthan Ernest did, we offered them a pound a week to take the children\r\nand bring them up as though they were their own. They jumped at the\r\noffer, and in another day or two we brought the children down and left\r\nthem, feeling that we had done as well as we could by them, at any rate\r\nfor the present. Then Ernest sent his small stock of goods to\r\nDebenham\xe2\x80\x99s, gave up the house he had taken two and a half years\r\npreviously, and returned to civilisation.\r\n\r\nI had expected that he would now rapidly recover, and was disappointed\r\nto see him get as I thought decidedly worse. Indeed, before long I\r\nthought him looking so ill that I insisted on his going with me to\r\nconsult one of the most eminent doctors in London. This gentleman said\r\nthere was no acute disease but that my young friend was suffering from\r\nnervous prostration, the result of long and severe mental suffering,\r\nfrom which there was no remedy except time, prosperity and rest.\r\n\r\nHe said that Ernest must have broken down later on, but that he might\r\nhave gone on for some months yet. It was the suddenness of the relief\r\nfrom tension which had knocked him over now.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCross him,\xe2\x80\x9d said the doctor, \xe2\x80\x9cat once. Crossing is the great medical\r\ndiscovery of the age. Shake him out of himself by shaking something\r\nelse into him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI had not told him that money was no object to us and I think he had\r\nreckoned me up as not over rich. He continued:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeeing is a mode of touching, touching is a mode of feeding, feeding\r\nis a mode of assimilation, assimilation is a mode of recreation and\r\nreproduction, and this is crossing\xe2\x80\x94shaking yourself into something else\r\nand something else into you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe spoke laughingly, but it was plain he was serious. He continued:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeople are always coming to me who want crossing, or change, if you\r\nprefer it, and who I know have not money enough to let them get away\r\nfrom London. This has set me thinking how I can best cross them even if\r\nthey cannot leave home, and I have made a list of cheap London\r\namusements which I recommend to my patients; none of them cost more\r\nthan a few shillings or take more than half a day or a day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI explained that there was no occasion to consider money in this case.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad of it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, still laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cThe homoeopathists use\r\n_aurum_ as a medicine, but they do not give it in large doses enough;\r\nif you can dose your young friend with this pretty freely you will soon\r\nbring him round. However, Mr Pontifex is not well enough to stand so\r\ngreat a change as going abroad yet; from what you tell me I should\r\nthink he had had as much change lately as is good for him. If he were\r\nto go abroad now he would probably be taken seriously ill within a\r\nweek. We must wait till he has recovered tone a little more. I will\r\nbegin by ringing my London changes on him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe thought a little and then said:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have found the Zoological Gardens of service to many of my patients.\r\nI should prescribe for Mr Pontifex a course of the larger mammals.\r\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t let him think he is taking them medicinally, but let him go to\r\ntheir house twice a week for a fortnight, and stay with the\r\nhippopotamus, the rhinoceros, and the elephants, till they begin to\r\nbore him. I find these beasts do my patients more good than any others.\r\nThe monkeys are not a wide enough cross; they do not stimulate\r\nsufficiently. The larger carnivora are unsympathetic. The reptiles are\r\nworse than useless, and the marsupials are not much better. Birds\r\nagain, except parrots, are not very beneficial; he may look at them now\r\nand again, but with the elephants and the pig tribe generally he should\r\nmix just now as freely as possible.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, you know, to prevent monotony I should send him, say, to morning\r\nservice at the Abbey before he goes. He need not stay longer than the\r\n_Te Deum_. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know why, but _Jubilates_ are seldom satisfactory.\r\nJust let him look in at the Abbey, and sit quietly in Poets\xe2\x80\x99 Corner\r\ntill the main part of the music is over. Let him do this two or three\r\ntimes, not more, before he goes to the Zoo.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen next day send him down to Gravesend by boat. By all means let him\r\ngo to the theatres in the evenings\xe2\x80\x94and then let him come to me again in\r\na fortnight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHad the doctor been less eminent in his profession I should have\r\ndoubted whether he was in earnest, but I knew him to be a man of\r\nbusiness who would neither waste his own time nor that of his patients.\r\nAs soon as we were out of the house we took a cab to Regent\xe2\x80\x99s Park, and\r\nspent a couple of hours in sauntering round the different houses.\r\nPerhaps it was on account of what the doctor had told me, but I\r\ncertainly became aware of a feeling I had never experienced before. I\r\nmean that I was receiving an influx of new life, or deriving new ways\r\nof looking at life\xe2\x80\x94which is the same thing\xe2\x80\x94by the process. I found the\r\ndoctor quite right in his estimate of the larger mammals as the ones\r\nwhich on the whole were most beneficial, and observed that Ernest, who\r\nhad heard nothing of what the doctor had said to me, lingered\r\ninstinctively in front of them. As for the elephants, especially the\r\nbaby elephant, he seemed to be drinking in large draughts of their\r\nlives to the re-creation and regeneration of his own.\r\n\r\nWe dined in the gardens, and I noticed with pleasure that Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nappetite was already improved. Since this time, whenever I have been a\r\nlittle out of sorts myself I have at once gone up to Regent\xe2\x80\x99s Park, and\r\nhave invariably been benefited. I mention this here in the hope that\r\nsome one or other of my readers may find the hint a useful one.\r\n\r\nAt the end of his fortnight my hero was much better, more so even than\r\nour friend the doctor had expected. \xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cMr Pontifex may go\r\nabroad, and the sooner the better. Let him stay a couple of months.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was the first Ernest had heard about his going abroad, and he\r\ntalked about my not being able to spare him for so long. I soon made\r\nthis all right.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is now the beginning of April,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, \xe2\x80\x9cgo down to Marseilles at\r\nonce, and take steamer to Nice. Then saunter down the Riviera to\r\nGenoa\xe2\x80\x94from Genoa go to Florence, Rome and Naples, and come home by way\r\nof Venice and the Italian lakes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd won\xe2\x80\x99t you come too?\xe2\x80\x9d said he, eagerly.\r\n\r\nI said I did not mind if I did, so we began to make our arrangements\r\nnext morning, and completed them within a very few days.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER LXXX\r\n\r\n\r\nWe left by the night mail, crossing from Dover. The night was soft, and\r\nthere was a bright moon upon the sea. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you love the smell of\r\ngrease about the engine of a Channel steamer? Isn\xe2\x80\x99t there a lot of hope\r\nin it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Ernest to me, for he had been to Normandy one summer as a\r\nboy with his father and mother, and the smell carried him back to days\r\nbefore those in which he had begun to bruise himself against the great\r\noutside world. \xe2\x80\x9cI always think one of the best parts of going abroad is\r\nthe first thud of the piston, and the first gurgling of the water when\r\nthe paddle begins to strike it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was very dreamy getting out at Calais, and trudging about with\r\nluggage in a foreign town at an hour when we were generally both of us\r\nin bed and fast asleep, but we settled down to sleep as soon as we got\r\ninto the railway carriage, and dozed till we had passed Amiens. Then\r\nwaking when the first signs of morning crispness were beginning to show\r\nthemselves, I saw that Ernest was already devouring every object we\r\npassed with quick sympathetic curiousness. There was not a peasant in a\r\nblouse driving his cart betimes along the road to market, not a\r\nsignalman\xe2\x80\x99s wife in her husband\xe2\x80\x99s hat and coat waving a green flag, not\r\na shepherd taking out his sheep to the dewy pastures, not a bank of\r\nopening cowslips as we passed through the railway cuttings, but he was\r\ndrinking it all in with an enjoyment too deep for words. The name of\r\nthe engine that drew us was Mozart, and Ernest liked this too.\r\n\r\nWe reached Paris by six, and had just time to get across the town and\r\ntake a morning express train to Marseilles, but before noon my young\r\nfriend was tired out and had resigned himself to a series of sleeps\r\nwhich were seldom intermitted for more than an hour or so together. He\r\nfought against this for a time, but in the end consoled himself by\r\nsaying it was so nice to have so much pleasure that he could afford to\r\nthrow a lot of it away. Having found a theory on which to justify\r\nhimself, he slept in peace.\r\n\r\nAt Marseilles we rested, and there the excitement of the change proved,\r\nas I had half feared it would, too much for my godson\xe2\x80\x99s still enfeebled\r\nstate. For a few days he was really ill, but after this he righted. For\r\nmy own part I reckon being ill as one of the great pleasures of life,\r\nprovided one is not too ill and is not obliged to work till one is\r\nbetter. I remember being ill once in a foreign hotel myself and how\r\nmuch I enjoyed it. To lie there careless of everything, quiet and warm,\r\nand with no weight upon the mind, to hear the clinking of the plates in\r\nthe far-off kitchen as the scullion rinsed them and put them by; to\r\nwatch the soft shadows come and go upon the ceiling as the sun came out\r\nor went behind a cloud; to listen to the pleasant murmuring of the\r\nfountain in the court below, and the shaking of the bells on the\r\nhorses\xe2\x80\x99 collars and the clink of their hoofs upon the ground as the\r\nflies plagued them; not only to be a lotus-eater but to know that it\r\nwas one\xe2\x80\x99s duty to be a lotus-eater. \xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d I thought to myself, \xe2\x80\x9cif I\r\ncould only now, having so forgotten care, drop off to sleep for ever,\r\nwould not this be a better piece of fortune than any I can ever hope\r\nfor?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOf course it would, but we would not take it though it were offered us.\r\nNo matter what evil may befall us, we will mostly abide by it and see\r\nit out.\r\n\r\nI could see that Ernest felt much as I had felt myself. He said little,\r\nbut noted everything. Once only did he frighten me. He called me to his\r\nbedside just as it was getting dusk and said in a grave, quiet manner\r\nthat he should like to speak to me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been thinking,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I may perhaps never recover from\r\nthis illness, and in case I do not I should like you to know that there\r\nis only one thing which weighs upon me. I refer,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued after a\r\nslight pause, \xe2\x80\x9cto my conduct towards my father and mother. I have been\r\nmuch too good to them. I treated them much too considerately,\xe2\x80\x9d on which\r\nhe broke into a smile which assured me that there was nothing seriously\r\namiss with him.\r\n\r\nOn the walls of his bedroom were a series of French Revolution prints\r\nrepresenting events in the life of Lycurgus. There was \xe2\x80\x9cGrandeur d\xe2\x80\x99\xc3\xa0me\r\nde Lycurgue,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cLycurgue consulte l\xe2\x80\x99oracle,\xe2\x80\x9d and then there was\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCalciope \xc3\xa0 la Cour.\xe2\x80\x9d Under this was written in French and Spanish:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMod\xc3\xa8le de gr\xc3\xa0ce et de beaut\xc3\xa9, la jeune Calciope non moins sage que\r\nbelle avait m\xc3\xa9rit\xc3\xa9 l\xe2\x80\x99estime et l\xe2\x80\x99attachement du vertueux Lycurgue.\r\nVivement \xc3\xa9pris de tant de charmes, l\xe2\x80\x99illustre philosophe la conduisait\r\ndans le temple de Junon, o\xc3\xb9 ils s\xe2\x80\x99unirent par un serment sacr\xc3\xa9. Apr\xc3\xa8s\r\ncette auguste c\xc3\xa9r\xc3\xa9monie, Lycurgue s\xe2\x80\x99empressa de conduire sa jeune\r\n\xc3\xa9pouse au palais de son fr\xc3\xa8re Polydecte, Roi de Lac\xc3\xa9d\xc3\xa9mon. Seigneur,\r\nlui dit-il, la vertueuse Calciope vient de recevoir mes voeux aux pieds\r\ndes autels, j\xe2\x80\x99ose vous prier d\xe2\x80\x99approuver cette union. Le Roi t\xc3\xa9moigna\r\nd\xe2\x80\x99abord quelque surprise, mais l\xe2\x80\x99estime qu\xe2\x80\x99il avait pour son fr\xc3\xa8re lui\r\ninspira une r\xc3\xa9ponse pleine de beinveillance. Il s\xe2\x80\x99approcha aussit\xc3\xb4t de\r\nCalciope qu\xe2\x80\x99il embrassa tendrement, combla ensuite Lycurgue de\r\npr\xc3\xa9venances et parut tr\xc3\xa8s satisfait.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe called my attention to this and then said somewhat timidly that he\r\nwould rather have married Ellen than Calciope. I saw he was hardening\r\nand made no hesitation about proposing that in another day or two we\r\nshould proceed upon our journey.\r\n\r\nI will not weary the reader by taking him with us over beaten ground.\r\nWe stopped at Siena, Cortona, Orvieto, Perugia and many other cities,\r\nand then after a fortnight passed between Rome and Naples went to the\r\nVenetian provinces and visited all those wondrous towns that lie\r\nbetween the southern slopes of the Alps and the northern ones of the\r\nApennines, coming back at last by the S. Gothard. I doubt whether he\r\nhad enjoyed the trip more than I did myself, but it was not till we\r\nwere on the point of returning that Ernest had recovered strength\r\nenough to be called fairly well, and it was not for many months that he\r\nso completely lost all sense of the wounds which the last four years\r\nhad inflicted on him as to feel as though there were a scar and a scar\r\nonly remaining.\r\n\r\nThey say that when people have lost an arm or a foot they feel pains in\r\nit now and again for a long while after they have lost it. One pain\r\nwhich he had almost forgotten came upon him on his return to England, I\r\nmean the sting of his having been imprisoned. As long as he was only a\r\nsmall shop-keeper his imprisonment mattered nothing; nobody knew of it,\r\nand if they had known they would not have cared; now, however, though\r\nhe was returning to his old position he was returning to it disgraced,\r\nand the pain from which he had been saved in the first instance by\r\nsurroundings so new that he had hardly recognised his own identity in\r\nthe middle of them, came on him as from a wound inflicted yesterday.\r\n\r\nHe thought of the high resolves which he had made in prison about using\r\nhis disgrace as a vantage ground of strength rather than trying to make\r\npeople forget it. \xe2\x80\x9cThat was all very well then,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought to himself,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwhen the grapes were beyond my reach, but now it is different.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nBesides, who but a prig would set himself high aims, or make high\r\nresolves at all?\r\n\r\nSome of his old friends, on learning that he had got rid of his\r\nsupposed wife and was now comfortably off again, wanted to renew their\r\nacquaintance; he was grateful to them and sometimes tried to meet their\r\nadvances half way, but it did not do, and ere long he shrank back into\r\nhimself, pretending not to know them. An infernal demon of honesty\r\nhaunted him which made him say to himself: \xe2\x80\x9cThese men know a great\r\ndeal, but do not know all\xe2\x80\x94if they did they would cut me\xe2\x80\x94and therefore I\r\nhave no right to their acquaintance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe thought that everyone except himself was _sans peur et sans\r\nreproche_. Of course they must be, for if they had not been, would they\r\nnot have been bound to warn all who had anything to do with them of\r\ntheir deficiencies? Well, he could not do this, and he would not have\r\npeople\xe2\x80\x99s acquaintance under false pretences, so he gave up even\r\nhankering after rehabilitation and fell back upon his old tastes for\r\nmusic and literature.\r\n\r\nOf course he has long since found out how silly all this was, how silly\r\nI mean in theory, for in practice it worked better than it ought to\r\nhave done, by keeping him free from _liaisons_ which would have tied\r\nhis tongue and made him see success elsewhere than where he came in\r\ntime to see it. He did what he did instinctively and for no other\r\nreason than because it was most natural to him. So far as he thought at\r\nall, he thought wrong, but what he did was right. I said something of\r\nthis kind to him once not so very long ago, and told him he had always\r\naimed high. \xe2\x80\x9cI never aimed at all,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied a little indignantly,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand you may be sure I should have aimed low enough if I had thought I\r\nhad got the chance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI suppose after all that no one whose mind was not, to put it mildly,\r\nabnormal, ever yet aimed very high out of pure malice aforethought. I\r\nonce saw a fly alight on a cup of hot coffee on which the milk had\r\nformed a thin skin; he perceived his extreme danger, and I noted with\r\nwhat ample strides and almost supermuscan effort he struck across the\r\ntreacherous surface and made for the edge of the cup\xe2\x80\x94for the ground was\r\nnot solid enough to let him raise himself from it by his wings. As I\r\nwatched him I fancied that so supreme a moment of difficulty and danger\r\nmight leave him with an increase of moral and physical power which\r\nmight even descend in some measure to his offspring. But surely he\r\nwould not have got the increased moral power if he could have helped\r\nit, and he will not knowingly alight upon another cup of hot coffee.\r\nThe more I see the more sure I am that it does not matter why people do\r\nthe right thing so long only as they do it, nor why they may have done\r\nthe wrong if they have done it. The result depends upon the thing done\r\nand the motive goes for nothing. I have read somewhere, but cannot\r\nremember where, that in some country district there was once a great\r\nscarcity of food, during which the poor suffered acutely; many indeed\r\nactually died of starvation, and all were hard put to it. In one\r\nvillage, however, there was a poor widow with a family of young\r\nchildren, who, though she had small visible means of subsistence, still\r\nlooked well-fed and comfortable, as also did all her little ones.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow,\xe2\x80\x9d everyone asked, \xe2\x80\x9cdid they manage to live?\xe2\x80\x9d It was plain they had\r\na secret, and it was equally plain that it could be no good one; for\r\nthere came a hurried, hunted look over the poor woman\xe2\x80\x99s face if anyone\r\nalluded to the way in which she and hers throve when others starved;\r\nthe family, moreover, were sometimes seen out at unusual hours of the\r\nnight, and evidently brought things home, which could hardly have been\r\nhonestly come by. They knew they were under suspicion, and, being\r\nhitherto of excellent name, it made them very unhappy, for it must be\r\nconfessed that they believed what they did to be uncanny if not\r\nabsolutely wicked; nevertheless, in spite of this they throve, and kept\r\ntheir strength when all their neighbours were pinched.\r\n\r\nAt length matters came to a head and the clergyman of the parish\r\ncross-questioned the poor woman so closely that with many tears and a\r\nbitter sense of degradation she confessed the truth; she and her\r\nchildren went into the hedges and gathered snails, which they made into\r\nbroth and ate\xe2\x80\x94could she ever be forgiven? Was there any hope of\r\nsalvation for her either in this world or the next after such unnatural\r\nconduct?\r\n\r\nSo again I have heard of an old dowager countess whose money was all in\r\nConsols; she had had many sons, and in her anxiety to give the younger\r\nones a good start, wanted a larger income than Consols would give her.\r\nShe consulted her solicitor and was advised to sell her Consols and\r\ninvest in the London and North-Western Railway, then at about 85. This\r\nwas to her what eating snails was to the poor widow whose story I have\r\ntold above. With shame and grief, as of one doing an unclean thing\xe2\x80\x94but\r\nher boys must have their start\xe2\x80\x94she did as she was advised. Then for a\r\nlong while she could not sleep at night and was haunted by a presage of\r\ndisaster. Yet what happened? She started her boys, and in a few years\r\nfound her capital doubled into the bargain, on which she sold out and\r\nwent back again to Consols and died in the full blessedness of\r\nfund-holding.\r\n\r\nShe thought, indeed, that she was doing a wrong and dangerous thing,\r\nbut this had absolutely nothing to do with it. Suppose she had invested\r\nin the full confidence of a recommendation by some eminent London\r\nbanker whose advice was bad, and so had lost all her money, and suppose\r\nshe had done this with a light heart and with no conviction of\r\nsin\xe2\x80\x94would her innocence of evil purpose and the excellence of her\r\nmotive have stood her in any stead? Not they.\r\n\r\nBut to return to my story. Towneley gave my hero most trouble.\r\nTowneley, as I have said, knew that Ernest would have money soon, but\r\nErnest did not of course know that he knew it. Towneley was rich\r\nhimself, and was married now; Ernest would be rich soon, had _bona\r\nfide_ intended to be married already, and would doubtless marry a\r\nlawful wife later on. Such a man was worth taking pains with, and when\r\nTowneley one day met Ernest in the street, and Ernest tried to avoid\r\nhim, Towneley would not have it, but with his usual quick good nature\r\nread his thoughts, caught him, morally speaking, by the scruff of his\r\nneck, and turned him laughingly inside out, telling him he would have\r\nno such nonsense.\r\n\r\nTowneley was just as much Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s idol now as he had ever been, and\r\nErnest, who was very easily touched, felt more gratefully and warmly\r\nthan ever towards him, but there was an unconscious something which was\r\nstronger than Towneley, and made my hero determine to break with him\r\nmore determinedly perhaps than with any other living person; he thanked\r\nhim in a low hurried voice and pressed his hand, while tears came into\r\nhis eyes in spite of all his efforts to repress them. \xe2\x80\x9cIf we meet\r\nagain,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cdo not look at me, but if hereafter you hear of me\r\nwriting things you do not like, think of me as charitably as you can,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nand so they parted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTowneley is a good fellow,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, gravely, \xe2\x80\x9cand you should not have\r\ncut him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTowneley,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, \xe2\x80\x9cis not only a good fellow, but he is without\r\nexception the very best man I ever saw in my life\xe2\x80\x94except,\xe2\x80\x9d he paid me\r\nthe compliment of saying, \xe2\x80\x9cyourself; Towneley is my notion of\r\neverything which I should most like to be\xe2\x80\x94but there is no real\r\nsolidarity between us. I should be in perpetual fear of losing his good\r\nopinion if I said things he did not like, and I mean to say a great\r\nmany things,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued more merrily, \xe2\x80\x9cwhich Towneley will not\r\nlike.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA man, as I have said already, can give up father and mother for\r\nChrist\xe2\x80\x99s sake tolerably easily for the most part, but it is not so easy\r\nto give up people like Towneley.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER LXXXI\r\n\r\n\r\nSo he fell away from all old friends except myself and three or four\r\nold intimates of my own, who were as sure to take to him as he to them,\r\nand who like myself enjoyed getting hold of a young fresh mind. Ernest\r\nattended to the keeping of my account books whenever there was anything\r\nwhich could possibly be attended to, which there seldom was, and spent\r\nthe greater part of the rest of his time in adding to the many notes\r\nand tentative essays which had already accumulated in his portfolios.\r\nAnyone who was used to writing could see at a glance that literature\r\nwas his natural development, and I was pleased at seeing him settle\r\ndown to it so spontaneously. I was less pleased, however, to observe\r\nthat he would still occupy himself with none but the most serious, I\r\nhad almost said solemn, subjects, just as he never cared about any but\r\nthe most serious kind of music.\r\n\r\nI said to him one day that the very slender reward which God had\r\nattached to the pursuit of serious inquiry was a sufficient proof that\r\nHe disapproved of it, or at any rate that He did not set much store by\r\nit nor wish to encourage it.\r\n\r\nHe said: \xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t talk about rewards. Look at Milton, who only got \xc2\xa35\r\nfor \xe2\x80\x98Paradise Lost.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd a great deal too much,\xe2\x80\x9d I rejoined promptly. \xe2\x80\x9cI would have given\r\nhim twice as much myself not to have written it at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nErnest was a little shocked. \xe2\x80\x9cAt any rate,\xe2\x80\x9d he said laughingly, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t write poetry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was a cut at me, for my burlesques were, of course, written in\r\nrhyme. So I dropped the matter.\r\n\r\nAfter a time he took it into his head to reopen the question of his\r\ngetting \xc2\xa3300 a year for doing, as he said, absolutely nothing, and said\r\nhe would try to find some employment which should bring him in enough\r\nto live upon.\r\n\r\nI laughed at this but let him alone. He tried and tried very hard for a\r\nlong while, but I need hardly say was unsuccessful. The older I grow,\r\nthe more convinced I become of the folly and credulity of the public;\r\nbut at the same time the harder do I see it is to impose oneself upon\r\nthat folly and credulity.\r\n\r\nHe tried editor after editor with article after article. Sometimes an\r\neditor listened to him and told him to leave his articles; he almost\r\ninvariably, however, had them returned to him in the end with a polite\r\nnote saying that they were not suited for the particular paper to which\r\nhe had sent them. And yet many of these very articles appeared in his\r\nlater works, and no one complained of them, not at least on the score\r\nof bad literary workmanship. \xe2\x80\x9cI see,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to me one day, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\r\ndemand is very imperious, and supply must be very suppliant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOnce, indeed, the editor of an important monthly magazine accepted an\r\narticle from him, and he thought he had now got a footing in the\r\nliterary world. The article was to appear in the next issue but one,\r\nand he was to receive proof from the printers in about ten days or a\r\nfortnight; but week after week passed and there was no proof; month\r\nafter month went by and there was still no room for Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s article;\r\nat length after about six months the editor one morning told him that\r\nhe had filled every number of his review for the next ten months, but\r\nthat his article should definitely appear. On this he insisted on\r\nhaving his MS. returned to him.\r\n\r\nSometimes his articles were actually published, and he found the editor\r\nhad edited them according to his own fancy, putting in jokes which he\r\nthought were funny, or cutting out the very passage which Ernest had\r\nconsidered the point of the whole thing, and then, though the articles\r\nappeared, when it came to paying for them it was another matter, and he\r\nnever saw his money. \xe2\x80\x9cEditors,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to me one day about this time,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9care like the people who bought and sold in the book of Revelation;\r\nthere is not one but has the mark of the beast upon him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt last after months of disappointment and many a tedious hour wasted\r\nin dingy anterooms (and of all anterooms those of editors appear to me\r\nto be the dreariest), he got a _bona fide_ offer of employment from one\r\nof the first class weekly papers through an introduction I was able to\r\nget for him from one who had powerful influence with the paper in\r\nquestion. The editor sent him a dozen long books upon varied and\r\ndifficult subjects, and told him to review them in a single article\r\nwithin a week. In one book there was an editorial note to the effect\r\nthat the writer was to be condemned. Ernest particularly admired the\r\nbook he was desired to condemn, and feeling how hopeless it was for him\r\nto do anything like justice to the books submitted to him, returned\r\nthem to the editor.\r\n\r\nAt last one paper did actually take a dozen or so of articles from him,\r\nand gave him cash down a couple of guineas apiece for them, but having\r\ndone this it expired within a fortnight after the last of Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s\r\narticles had appeared. It certainly looked very much as if the other\r\neditors knew their business in declining to have anything to do with my\r\nunlucky godson.\r\n\r\nI was not sorry that he failed with periodical literature, for writing\r\nfor reviews or newspapers is bad training for one who may aspire to\r\nwrite works of more permanent interest. A young writer should have more\r\ntime for reflection than he can get as a contributor to the daily or\r\neven weekly press. Ernest himself, however, was chagrined at finding\r\nhow unmarketable he was. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to me, \xe2\x80\x9cIf I was a well-bred\r\nhorse, or sheep, or a pure-bred pigeon or lop-eared rabbit I should be\r\nmore saleable. If I was even a cathedral in a colonial town people\r\nwould give me something, but as it is they do not want me\xe2\x80\x9d; and now\r\nthat he was well and rested he wanted to set up a shop again, but this,\r\nof course, I would not hear of.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat care I,\xe2\x80\x9d said he to me one day, \xe2\x80\x9cabout being what they call a\r\ngentleman?\xe2\x80\x9d And his manner was almost fierce.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat has being a gentleman ever done for me except make me less able\r\nto prey and more easy to be preyed upon? It has changed the manner of\r\nmy being swindled, that is all. But for your kindness to me I should be\r\npenniless. Thank heaven I have placed my children where I have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI begged him to keep quiet a little longer and not talk about taking a\r\nshop.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill being a gentleman,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cbring me money at the last, and\r\nwill anything bring me as much peace at the last as money will? They\r\nsay that those who have riches enter hardly into the kingdom of Heaven.\r\nBy Jove, they do; they are like Struldbrugs; they live and live and\r\nlive and are happy for many a long year after they would have entered\r\ninto the kingdom of Heaven if they had been poor. I want to live long\r\nand to raise my children, if I see they would be happier for the\r\nraising; that is what I want, and it is not what I am doing now that\r\nwill help me. Being a gentleman is a luxury which I cannot afford,\r\ntherefore I do not want it. Let me go back to my shop again, and do\r\nthings for people which they want done and will pay me for doing for\r\nthem. They know what they want and what is good for them better than I\r\ncan tell them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was hard to deny the soundness of this, and if he had been dependent\r\nonly on the \xc2\xa3300 a year which he was getting from me I should have\r\nadvised him to open his shop again next morning. As it was, I\r\ntemporised and raised obstacles, and quieted him from time to time as\r\nbest I could.\r\n\r\nOf course he read Mr Darwin\xe2\x80\x99s books as fast as they came out and\r\nadopted evolution as an article of faith. \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said\r\nonce, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I am like one of those caterpillars which, if they have\r\nbeen interrupted in making their hammock, must begin again from the\r\nbeginning. So long as I went back a long way down in the social scale I\r\ngot on all right, and should have made money but for Ellen; when I try\r\nto take up the work at a higher stage I fail completely.\xe2\x80\x9d I do not know\r\nwhether the analogy holds good or not, but I am sure Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s instinct\r\nwas right in telling him that after a heavy fall he had better begin\r\nlife again at a very low stage, and as I have just said, I would have\r\nlet him go back to his shop if I had not known what I did.\r\n\r\nAs the time fixed upon by his aunt drew nearer I prepared him more and\r\nmore for what was coming, and at last, on his twenty-eighth birthday, I\r\nwas able to tell him all and to show him the letter signed by his aunt\r\nupon her death-bed to the effect that I was to hold the money in trust\r\nfor him. His birthday happened that year (1863) to be on a Sunday, but\r\non the following day I transferred his shares into his own name, and\r\npresented him with the account books which he had been keeping for the\r\nlast year and a half.\r\n\r\nIn spite of all that I had done to prepare him, it was a long while\r\nbefore I could get him actually to believe that the money was his own.\r\nHe did not say much\xe2\x80\x94no more did I, for I am not sure that I did not\r\nfeel as much moved at having brought my long trusteeship to a\r\nsatisfactory conclusion as Ernest did at finding himself owner of more\r\nthan \xc2\xa370,000. When he did speak it was to jerk out a sentence or two of\r\nreflection at a time. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I were rendering this moment in music,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cI should allow myself free use of the augmented sixth.\xe2\x80\x9d A little\r\nlater I remember his saying with a laugh that had something of a family\r\nlikeness to his aunt\xe2\x80\x99s: \xe2\x80\x9cIt is not the pleasure it causes me which I\r\nenjoy so, it is the pain it will cause to all my friends except\r\nyourself and Towneley.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI said: \xe2\x80\x9cYou cannot tell your father and mother\xe2\x80\x94it would drive them\r\nmad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, no,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cit would be too cruel; it would be like Isaac\r\noffering up Abraham and no thicket with a ram in it near at hand.\r\nBesides why should I? We have cut each other these four years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER LXXXII\r\n\r\n\r\nIt almost seemed as though our casual mention of Theobald and Christina\r\nhad in some way excited them from a dormant to an active state. During\r\nthe years that had elapsed since they last appeared upon the scene they\r\nhad remained at Battersby, and had concentrated their affection upon\r\ntheir other children.\r\n\r\nIt had been a bitter pill to Theobald to lose his power of plaguing his\r\nfirst-born; if the truth were known I believe he had felt this more\r\nacutely than any disgrace which might have been shed upon him by\r\nErnest\xe2\x80\x99s imprisonment. He had made one or two attempts to reopen\r\nnegotiations through me, but I never said anything about them to\r\nErnest, for I knew it would upset him. I wrote, however, to Theobald\r\nthat I had found his son inexorable, and recommended him for the\r\npresent, at any rate, to desist from returning to the subject. This I\r\nthought would be at once what Ernest would like best and Theobald\r\nleast.\r\n\r\nA few days, however, after Ernest had come into his property, I\r\nreceived a letter from Theobald enclosing one for Ernest which I could\r\nnot withhold.\r\n\r\nThe letter ran thus:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo my son Ernest,\xe2\x80\x94Although you have more than once rejected my\r\novertures I appeal yet again to your better nature. Your mother, who\r\nhas long been ailing, is, I believe, near her end; she is unable to\r\nkeep anything on her stomach, and Dr Martin holds out but little hopes\r\nof her recovery. She has expressed a wish to see you, and says she\r\nknows you will not refuse to come to her, which, considering her\r\ncondition, I am unwilling to suppose you will.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI remit you a Post Office order for your fare, and will pay your\r\nreturn journey.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you want clothes to come in, order what you consider suitable, and\r\ndesire that the bill be sent to me; I will pay it immediately, to an\r\namount not exceeding eight or nine pounds, and if you will let me know\r\nwhat train you will come by, I will send the carriage to meet you.\r\nBelieve me, Your affectionate father,\r\n\r\n\r\nT. PONTIFEX.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOf course there could be no hesitation on Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s part. He could\r\nafford to smile now at his father\xe2\x80\x99s offering to pay for his clothes,\r\nand his sending him a Post Office order for the exact price of a\r\nsecond-class ticket, and he was of course shocked at learning the state\r\nhis mother was said to be in, and touched at her desire to see him. He\r\ntelegraphed that he would come down at once. I saw him a little before\r\nhe started, and was pleased to see how well his tailor had done by him.\r\nTowneley himself could not have been appointed more becomingly. His\r\nportmanteau, his railway wrapper, everything he had about him, was in\r\nkeeping. I thought he had grown much better-looking than he had been at\r\ntwo or three and twenty. His year and a half of peace had effaced all\r\nthe ill effects of his previous suffering, and now that he had become\r\nactually rich there was an air of _insouciance_ and good humour upon\r\nhis face, as of a man with whom everything was going perfectly right,\r\nwhich would have made a much plainer man good-looking. I was proud of\r\nhim and delighted with him. \xe2\x80\x9cI am sure,\xe2\x80\x9d I said to myself, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\r\nwhatever else he may do, he will never marry again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe journey was a painful one. As he drew near to the station and\r\ncaught sight of each familiar feature, so strong was the force of\r\nassociation that he felt as though his coming into his aunt\xe2\x80\x99s money had\r\nbeen a dream, and he were again returning to his father\xe2\x80\x99s house as he\r\nhad returned to it from Cambridge for the vacations. Do what he would,\r\nthe old dull weight of _home-sickness_ began to oppress him, his heart\r\nbeat fast as he thought of his approaching meeting with his father and\r\nmother, \xe2\x80\x9cand I shall have,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cto kiss Charlotte.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWould his father meet him at the station? Would he greet him as though\r\nnothing had happened, or would he be cold and distant? How, again,\r\nwould he take the news of his son\xe2\x80\x99s good fortune? As the train drew up\r\nto the platform, Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s eye ran hurriedly over the few people who\r\nwere in the station. His father\xe2\x80\x99s well-known form was not among them,\r\nbut on the other side of the palings which divided the station yard\r\nfrom the platform, he saw the pony carriage, looking, as he thought,\r\nrather shabby, and recognised his father\xe2\x80\x99s coachman. In a few minutes\r\nmore he was in the carriage driving towards Battersby. He could not\r\nhelp smiling as he saw the coachman give a look of surprise at finding\r\nhim so much changed in personal appearance. The coachman was the more\r\nsurprised because when Ernest had last been at home he had been dressed\r\nas a clergyman, and now he was not only a layman, but a layman who was\r\ngot up regardless of expense. The change was so great that it was not\r\ntill Ernest actually spoke to him that the coachman knew him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow are my father and mother?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked hurriedly, as he got into the\r\ncarriage. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Master\xe2\x80\x99s well, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d was the answer, \xe2\x80\x9cbut the Missis is\r\nvery sadly.\xe2\x80\x9d The horse knew that he was going home and pulled hard at\r\nthe reins. The weather was cold and raw\xe2\x80\x94the very ideal of a November\r\nday; in one part of the road the floods were out, and near here they\r\nhad to pass through a number of horsemen and dogs, for the hounds had\r\nmet that morning at a place near Battersby. Ernest saw several people\r\nwhom he knew, but they either, as is most likely, did not recognise\r\nhim, or did not know of his good luck. When Battersby church tower drew\r\nnear, and he saw the Rectory on the top of the hill, its chimneys just\r\nshowing above the leafless trees with which it was surrounded, he threw\r\nhimself back in the carriage and covered his face with his hands.\r\n\r\nIt came to an end, as even the worst quarters of an hour do, and in a\r\nfew minutes more he was on the steps in front of his father\xe2\x80\x99s house.\r\nHis father, hearing the carriage arrive, came a little way down the\r\nsteps to meet him. Like the coachman he saw at a glance that Ernest was\r\nappointed as though money were abundant with him, and that he was\r\nlooking robust and full of health and vigour.\r\n\r\nThis was not what he had bargained for. He wanted Ernest to return, but\r\nhe was to return as any respectable, well-regulated prodigal ought to\r\nreturn\xe2\x80\x94abject, broken-hearted, asking forgiveness from the tenderest\r\nand most long-suffering father in the whole world. If he should have\r\nshoes and stockings and whole clothes at all, it should be only because\r\nabsolute rags and tatters had been graciously dispensed with, whereas\r\nhere he was swaggering in a grey ulster and a blue and white necktie,\r\nand looking better than Theobald had ever seen him in his life. It was\r\nunprincipled. Was it for this that he had been generous enough to offer\r\nto provide Ernest with decent clothes in which to come and visit his\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x99s death-bed? Could any advantage be meaner than the one which\r\nErnest had taken? Well, he would not go a penny beyond the eight or\r\nnine pounds which he had promised. It was fortunate he had given a\r\nlimit. Why he, Theobald, had never been able to afford such a\r\nportmanteau in his life. He was still using an old one which his father\r\nhad turned over to him when he went up to Cambridge. Besides, he had\r\nsaid clothes, not a portmanteau.\r\n\r\nErnest saw what was passing through his father\xe2\x80\x99s mind, and felt that he\r\nought to have prepared him in some way for what he now saw; but he had\r\nsent his telegram so immediately on receiving his father\xe2\x80\x99s letter, and\r\nhad followed it so promptly that it would not have been easy to do so\r\neven if he had thought of it. He put out his hand and said laughingly,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s all paid for\xe2\x80\x94I am afraid you do not know that Mr Overton has\r\nhanded over to me Aunt Alethea\xe2\x80\x99s money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTheobald flushed scarlet. \xe2\x80\x9cBut why,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, and these were the first\r\nwords that actually crossed his lips\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cif the money was not his to keep,\r\ndid he not hand it over to my brother John and me?\xe2\x80\x9d He stammered a good\r\ndeal and looked sheepish, but he got the words out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause, my dear father,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ernest still laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cmy aunt left it\r\nto him in trust for me, not in trust either for you or for my Uncle\r\nJohn\xe2\x80\x94and it has accumulated till it is now over \xc2\xa370,000. But tell me\r\nhow is my mother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Ernest,\xe2\x80\x9d said Theobald excitedly, \xe2\x80\x9cthe matter cannot rest here, I\r\nmust know that this is all open and above board.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis had the true Theobald ring and instantly brought the whole train\r\nof ideas which in Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s mind were connected with his father. The\r\nsurroundings were the old familiar ones, but the surrounded were\r\nchanged almost beyond power of recognition. He turned sharply on\r\nTheobald in a moment. I will not repeat the words he used, for they\r\ncame out before he had time to consider them, and they might strike\r\nsome of my readers as disrespectful; there were not many of them, but\r\nthey were effectual. Theobald said nothing, but turned almost of an\r\nashen colour; he never again spoke to his son in such a way as to make\r\nit necessary for him to repeat what he had said on this occasion.\r\nErnest quickly recovered his temper and again asked after his mother.\r\nTheobald was glad enough to take this opening now, and replied at once\r\nin the tone he would have assumed towards one he most particularly\r\ndesired to conciliate, that she was getting rapidly worse in spite of\r\nall he had been able to do for her, and concluded by saying she had\r\nbeen the comfort and mainstay of his life for more than thirty years,\r\nbut that he could not wish it prolonged.\r\n\r\nThe pair then went upstairs to Christina\xe2\x80\x99s room, the one in which\r\nErnest had been born. His father went before him and prepared her for\r\nher son\xe2\x80\x99s approach. The poor woman raised herself in bed as he came\r\ntowards her, and weeping as she flung her arms around him, cried: \xe2\x80\x9cOh,\r\nI knew he would come, I knew, I knew he could come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nErnest broke down and wept as he had not done for years.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my boy, my boy,\xe2\x80\x9d she said as soon as she could recover her voice.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you never really been near us for all these years? Ah, you do not\r\nknow how we have loved you and mourned over you, papa just as much as I\r\nhave. You know he shows his feelings less, but I can never tell you how\r\nvery, very deeply he has felt for you. Sometimes at night I have\r\nthought I have heard footsteps in the garden, and have got quietly out\r\nof bed lest I should wake him, and gone to the window to look out, but\r\nthere has been only dark or the greyness of the morning, and I have\r\ngone crying back to bed again. Still I think you have been near us\r\nthough you were too proud to let us know\xe2\x80\x94and now at last I have you in\r\nmy arms once more, my dearest, dearest boy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHow cruel, how infamously unfeeling Ernest thought he had been.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMother,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cforgive me\xe2\x80\x94the fault was mine, I ought not to have\r\nbeen so hard; I was wrong, very wrong\xe2\x80\x9d; the poor blubbering fellow\r\nmeant what he said, and his heart yearned to his mother as he had never\r\nthought that it could yearn again. \xe2\x80\x9cBut have you never,\xe2\x80\x9d she continued,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ccome although it was in the dark and we did not know it\xe2\x80\x94oh, let me\r\nthink that you have not been so cruel as we have thought you. Tell me\r\nthat you came if only to comfort me and make me happier.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nErnest was ready. \xe2\x80\x9cI had no money to come with, mother, till just\r\nlately.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was an excuse Christina could understand and make allowance for;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, then you would have come, and I will take the will for the\r\ndeed\xe2\x80\x94and now that I have you safe again, say that you will never, never\r\nleave me\xe2\x80\x94not till\xe2\x80\x94not till\xe2\x80\x94oh, my boy, have they told you I am dying?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nShe wept bitterly, and buried her head in her pillow.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER LXXXIII\r\n\r\n\r\nJoey and Charlotte were in the room. Joey was now ordained, and was\r\ncurate to Theobald. He and Ernest had never been sympathetic, and\r\nErnest saw at a glance that there was no chance of a _rapprochement_\r\nbetween them. He was a little startled at seeing Joey dressed as a\r\nclergyman, and looking so like what he had looked himself a few years\r\nearlier, for there was a good deal of family likeness between the pair;\r\nbut Joey\xe2\x80\x99s face was cold and was illumined with no spark of\r\nBohemianism; he was a clergyman and was going to do as other clergymen\r\ndid, neither better nor worse. He greeted Ernest rather _de haut en\r\nbas_, that is to say he began by trying to do so, but the affair tailed\r\noff unsatisfactorily.\r\n\r\nHis sister presented her cheek to him to be kissed. How he hated it; he\r\nhad been dreading it for the last three hours. She, too, was distant\r\nand reproachful in her manner, as such a superior person was sure to\r\nbe. She had a grievance against him inasmuch as she was still\r\nunmarried. She laid the blame of this at Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s door; it was his\r\nmisconduct she maintained in secret, which had prevented young men from\r\nmaking offers to her, and she ran him up a heavy bill for consequential\r\ndamages. She and Joey had from the first developed an instinct for\r\nhunting with the hounds, and now these two had fairly identified\r\nthemselves with the older generation\xe2\x80\x94that is to say as against Ernest.\r\nOn this head there was an offensive and defensive alliance between\r\nthem, but between themselves there was subdued but internecine warfare.\r\n\r\nThis at least was what Ernest gathered, partly from his recollections\r\nof the parties concerned, and partly from his observation of their\r\nlittle ways during the first half-hour after his arrival, while they\r\nwere all together in his mother\xe2\x80\x99s bedroom\xe2\x80\x94for as yet of course they did\r\nnot know that he had money. He could see that they eyed him from time\r\nto time with a surprise not unmixed with indignation, and knew very\r\nwell what they were thinking.\r\n\r\nChristina saw the change which had come over him\xe2\x80\x94how much firmer and\r\nmore vigorous both in mind and body he seemed than when she had last\r\nseen him. She saw too how well he was dressed, and, like the others, in\r\nspite of the return of all her affection for her first-born, was a\r\nlittle alarmed about Theobald\xe2\x80\x99s pocket, which she supposed would have\r\nto be mulcted for all this magnificence. Perceiving this, Ernest\r\nrelieved her mind and told her all about his aunt\xe2\x80\x99s bequest, and how I\r\nhad husbanded it, in the presence of his brother and sister\xe2\x80\x94who,\r\nhowever, pretended not to notice, or at any rate to notice as a matter\r\nin which they could hardly be expected to take an interest.\r\n\r\nHis mother kicked a little at first against the money\xe2\x80\x99s having gone to\r\nhim as she said \xe2\x80\x9cover his papa\xe2\x80\x99s head.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d she said in a\r\ndeprecating tone, \xe2\x80\x9cthis is more than ever your papa has had\xe2\x80\x9d; but\r\nErnest calmed her by suggesting that if Miss Pontifex had known how\r\nlarge the sum would become she would have left the greater part of it\r\nto Theobald. This compromise was accepted by Christina who forthwith,\r\nill as she was, entered with ardour into the new position, and taking\r\nit as a fresh point of departure, began spending Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s money for\r\nhim.\r\n\r\nI may say in passing that Christina was right in saying that Theobald\r\nhad never had so much money as his son was now possessed of. In the\r\nfirst place he had not had a fourteen years\xe2\x80\x99 minority with no outgoings\r\nto prevent the accumulation of the money, and in the second he, like\r\nmyself and almost everyone else, had suffered somewhat in the 1846\r\ntimes\xe2\x80\x94not enough to cripple him or even seriously to hurt him, but\r\nenough to give him a scare and make him stick to debentures for the\r\nrest of his life. It was the fact of his son\xe2\x80\x99s being the richer man of\r\nthe two, and of his being rich so young, which rankled with Theobald\r\neven more than the fact of his having money at all. If he had had to\r\nwait till he was sixty or sixty-five, and become broken down from long\r\nfailure in the meantime, why then perhaps he might have been allowed to\r\nhave whatever sum should suffice to keep him out of the workhouse and\r\npay his death-bed expenses; but that he should come in to \xc2\xa370,000 at\r\neight and twenty, and have no wife and only two children\xe2\x80\x94it was\r\nintolerable. Christina was too ill and in too great a hurry to spend\r\nthe money to care much about such details as the foregoing, and she was\r\nnaturally much more good-natured than Theobald.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis piece of good fortune\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94she saw it at a glance\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cquite wiped out\r\nthe disgrace of his having been imprisoned. There should be no more\r\nnonsense about that. The whole thing was a mistake, an unfortunate\r\nmistake, true, but the less said about it now the better. Of course\r\nErnest would come back and live at Battersby until he was married, and\r\nhe would pay his father handsomely for board and lodging. In fact it\r\nwould be only right that Theobald should make a profit, nor would\r\nErnest himself wish it to be other than a handsome one; this was far\r\nthe best and simplest arrangement; and he could take his sister out\r\nmore than Theobald or Joey cared to do, and would also doubtless\r\nentertain very handsomely at Battersby.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course he would buy Joey a living, and make large presents yearly\r\nto his sister\xe2\x80\x94was there anything else? Oh! yes\xe2\x80\x94he would become a county\r\nmagnate now; a man with nearly \xc2\xa34000 a year should certainly become a\r\ncounty magnate. He might even go into Parliament. He had very fair\r\nabilities, nothing indeed approaching such genius as Dr Skinner\xe2\x80\x99s, nor\r\neven as Theobald\xe2\x80\x99s, still he was not deficient and if he got into\r\nParliament\xe2\x80\x94so young too\xe2\x80\x94there was nothing to hinder his being Prime\r\nMinister before he died, and if so, of course, he would become a peer.\r\nOh! why did he not set about it all at once, so that she might live to\r\nhear people call her son \xe2\x80\x98my lord\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94Lord Battersby she thought would do\r\nvery nicely, and if she was well enough to sit he must certainly have\r\nher portrait painted at full length for one end of his large\r\ndining-hall.
