It should be exhibited at the Royal Academy: \xe2\x80\x98Portrait of\r\nLord Battersby\xe2\x80\x99s mother,\xe2\x80\x99 she said to herself, and her heart fluttered\r\nwith all its wonted vivacity. If she could not sit, happily, she had\r\nbeen photographed not so very long ago, and the portrait had been as\r\nsuccessful as any photograph could be of a face which depended so\r\nentirely upon its expression as her own. Perhaps the painter could take\r\nthe portrait sufficiently from this. It was better after all that\r\nErnest had given up the Church\xe2\x80\x94how far more wisely God arranges matters\r\nfor us than ever we can do for ourselves! She saw it all now\xe2\x80\x94it was\r\nJoey who would become Archbishop of Canterbury and Ernest would remain\r\na layman and become Prime Minister\xe2\x80\x9d . . . and so on till her daughter\r\ntold her it was time to take her medicine.\r\n\r\nI suppose this reverie, which is a mere fragment of what actually ran\r\nthrough Christina\xe2\x80\x99s brain, occupied about a minute and a half, but it,\r\nor the presence of her son, seemed to revive her spirits wonderfully.\r\nIll, dying indeed, and suffering as she was, she brightened up so as to\r\nlaugh once or twice quite merrily during the course of the afternoon.\r\nNext day Dr Martin said she was so much better that he almost began to\r\nhave hopes of her recovery again. Theobald, whenever this was touched\r\nupon as possible, would shake his head and say: \xe2\x80\x9cWe can\xe2\x80\x99t wish it\r\nprolonged,\xe2\x80\x9d and then Charlotte caught Ernest unawares and said: \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nknow, dear Ernest, that these ups and downs of talk are terribly\r\nagitating to papa; he could stand whatever comes, but it is quite too\r\nwearing to him to think half-a-dozen different things backwards and\r\nforwards, up and down in the same twenty-four hours, and it would be\r\nkinder of you not to do it\xe2\x80\x94I mean not to say anything to him even\r\nthough Dr Martin does hold out hopes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCharlotte had meant to imply that it was Ernest who was at the bottom\r\nof all the inconvenience felt by Theobald, herself, Joey and everyone\r\nelse, and she had actually got words out which should convey this;\r\ntrue, she had not dared to stick to them and had turned them off, but\r\nshe had made them hers at any rate for one brief moment, and this was\r\nbetter than nothing. Ernest noticed throughout his mother\xe2\x80\x99s illness,\r\nthat Charlotte found immediate occasion to make herself disagreeable to\r\nhim whenever either doctor or nurse pronounced her mother to be a\r\nlittle better. When she wrote to Crampsford to desire the prayers of\r\nthe congregation (she was sure her mother would wish it, and that the\r\nCrampsford people would be pleased at her remembrance of them), she was\r\nsending another letter on some quite different subject at the same\r\ntime, and put the two letters into the wrong envelopes. Ernest was\r\nasked to take these letters to the village post-office, and imprudently\r\ndid so; when the error came to be discovered Christina happened to have\r\nrallied a little. Charlotte flew at Ernest immediately, and laid all\r\nthe blame of the blunder upon his shoulders.\r\n\r\nExcept that Joey and Charlotte were more fully developed, the house and\r\nits inmates, organic and inorganic, were little changed since Ernest\r\nhad last seen them. The furniture and the ornaments on the\r\nchimney-piece were just as they had been ever since he could remember\r\nanything at all. In the drawing-room, on either side of the fireplace\r\nthere hung the Carlo Dolci and the Sassoferrato as in old times; there\r\nwas the water colour of a scene on the Lago Maggiore, copied by\r\nCharlotte from an original lent her by her drawing master, and finished\r\nunder his direction. This was the picture of which one of the servants\r\nhad said that it must be good, for Mr Pontifex had given ten shillings\r\nfor the frame. The paper on the walls was unchanged; the roses were\r\nstill waiting for the bees; and the whole family still prayed night and\r\nmorning to be made \xe2\x80\x9ctruly honest and conscientious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOne picture only was removed\xe2\x80\x94a photograph of himself which had hung\r\nunder one of his father and between those of his brother and sister.\r\nErnest noticed this at prayer time, while his father was reading about\r\nNoah\xe2\x80\x99s ark and how they daubed it with slime, which, as it happened,\r\nhad been Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s favourite text when he was a boy. Next morning,\r\nhowever, the photograph had found its way back again, a little dusty\r\nand with a bit of the gilding chipped off from one corner of the frame,\r\nbut there sure enough it was. I suppose they put it back when they\r\nfound how rich he had become.\r\n\r\nIn the dining-room the ravens were still trying to feed Elijah over the\r\nfireplace; what a crowd of reminiscences did not this picture bring\r\nback! Looking out of the window, there were the flower beds in the\r\nfront garden exactly as they had been, and Ernest found himself looking\r\nhard against the blue door at the bottom of the garden to see if there\r\nwas rain falling, as he had been used to look when he was a child doing\r\nlessons with his father.\r\n\r\nAfter their early dinner, when Joey and Ernest and their father were\r\nleft alone, Theobald rose and stood in the middle of the hearthrug\r\nunder the Elijah picture, and began to whistle in his old absent way.\r\nHe had two tunes only, one was \xe2\x80\x9cIn my Cottage near a Wood,\xe2\x80\x9d and the\r\nother was the Easter Hymn; he had been trying to whistle them all his\r\nlife, but had never succeeded; he whistled them as a clever bullfinch\r\nmight whistle them\xe2\x80\x94he had got them, but he had not got them right; he\r\nwould be a semitone out in every third note as though reverting to some\r\nremote musical progenitor, who had known none but the Lydian or the\r\nPhrygian mode, or whatever would enable him to go most wrong while\r\nstill keeping the tune near enough to be recognised. Theobald stood\r\nbefore the middle of the fire and whistled his two tunes softly in his\r\nown old way till Ernest left the room; the unchangedness of the\r\nexternal and changedness of the internal he felt were likely to throw\r\nhim completely off his balance.\r\n\r\nHe strolled out of doors into the sodden spinney behind the house, and\r\nsolaced himself with a pipe. Ere long he found himself at the door of\r\nthe cottage of his father\xe2\x80\x99s coachman, who had married an old lady\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmaid of his mother\xe2\x80\x99s, to whom Ernest had been always much attached as\r\nshe also to him, for she had known him ever since he had been five or\r\nsix years old. Her name was Susan. He sat down in the rocking-chair\r\nbefore her fire, and Susan went on ironing at the table in front of the\r\nwindow, and a smell of hot flannel pervaded the kitchen.\r\n\r\nSusan had been retained too securely by Christina to be likely to side\r\nwith Ernest all in a moment. He knew this very well, and did not call\r\non her for the sake of support, moral or otherwise. He had called\r\nbecause he liked her, and also because he knew that he should gather\r\nmuch in a chat with her that he should not be able to arrive at in any\r\nother way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Master Ernest,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy did you not come back when your\r\npoor papa and mamma wanted you? I\xe2\x80\x99m sure your ma has said to me a\r\nhundred times over if she has said it once that all should be exactly\r\nas it had been before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nErnest smiled to himself. It was no use explaining to Susan why he\r\nsmiled, so he said nothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the first day or two I thought she never would get over it; she\r\nsaid it was a judgement upon her, and went on about things as she had\r\nsaid and done many years ago, before your pa knew her, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nwhat she didn\xe2\x80\x99t say or wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have said only I stopped her; she\r\nseemed out of her mind like, and said that none of the neighbours would\r\never speak to her again, but the next day Mrs Bushby (her that was Miss\r\nCowey, you know) called, and your ma always was so fond of her, and it\r\nseemed to do her a power o\xe2\x80\x99 good, for the next day she went through all\r\nher dresses, and we settled how she should have them altered; and then\r\nall the neighbours called for miles and miles round, and your ma came\r\nin here, and said she had been going through the waters of misery, and\r\nthe Lord had turned them to a well.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Oh yes, Susan,\xe2\x80\x99 said she, \xe2\x80\x98be sure it is so. Whom the Lord loveth he\r\nchasteneth, Susan,\xe2\x80\x99 and here she began to cry again. \xe2\x80\x98As for him,\xe2\x80\x99 she\r\nwent on, \xe2\x80\x98he has made his bed, and he must lie on it; when he comes out\r\nof prison his pa will know what is best to be done, and Master Ernest\r\nmay be thankful that he has a pa so good and so long-suffering.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen when you would not see them, that was a cruel blow to your ma.\r\nYour pa did not say anything; you know your pa never does say very much\r\nunless he\xe2\x80\x99s downright waxy for the time; but your ma took on dreadful\r\nfor a few days, and I never saw the master look so black; but, bless\r\nyou, it all went off in a few days, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that there\xe2\x80\x99s been\r\nmuch difference in either of them since then, not till your ma was took\r\nill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn the night of his arrival he had behaved well at family prayers, as\r\nalso on the following morning; his father read about David\xe2\x80\x99s dying\r\ninjunctions to Solomon in the matter of Shimei, but he did not mind it.\r\nIn the course of the day, however, his corns had been trodden on so\r\nmany times that he was in a misbehaving humour, on this the second\r\nnight after his arrival. He knelt next Charlotte and said the responses\r\nperfunctorily, not so perfunctorily that she should know for certain\r\nthat he was doing it maliciously, but so perfunctorily as to make her\r\nuncertain whether he might be malicious or not, and when he had to pray\r\nto be made truly honest and conscientious he emphasised the \xe2\x80\x9ctruly.\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\ndo not know whether Charlotte noticed anything, but she knelt at some\r\ndistance from him during the rest of his stay. He assures me that this\r\nwas the only spiteful thing he did during the whole time he was at\r\nBattersby.\r\n\r\nWhen he went up to his bedroom, in which, to do them justice, they had\r\ngiven him a fire, he noticed what indeed he had noticed as soon as he\r\nwas shown into it on his arrival, that there was an illuminated card\r\nframed and glazed over his bed with the words, \xe2\x80\x9cBe the day weary or be\r\nthe day long, at last it ringeth to evensong.\xe2\x80\x9d He wondered to himself\r\nhow such people could leave such a card in a room in which their\r\nvisitors would have to spend the last hours of their evening, but he\r\nlet it alone. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s not enough difference between \xe2\x80\x98weary\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98long\xe2\x80\x99\r\nto warrant an \xe2\x80\x98or,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I suppose it is all right.\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nbelieve Christina had bought the card at a bazaar in aid of the\r\nrestoration of a neighbouring church, and having been bought it had got\r\nto be used\xe2\x80\x94besides, the sentiment was so touching and the illumination\r\nwas really lovely. Anyhow, no irony could be more complete than leaving\r\nit in my hero\xe2\x80\x99s bedroom, though assuredly no irony had been intended.\r\n\r\nOn the third day after Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s arrival Christina relapsed again. For\r\nthe last two days she had been in no pain and had slept a good deal;\r\nher son\xe2\x80\x99s presence still seemed to cheer her, and she often said how\r\nthankful she was to be surrounded on her death-bed by a family so\r\nhappy, so God-fearing, so united, but now she began to wander, and,\r\nbeing more sensible of the approach of death, seemed also more alarmed\r\nat the thoughts of the Day of Judgment.\r\n\r\nShe ventured more than once or twice to return to the subject of her\r\nsins, and implored Theobald to make quite sure that they were forgiven\r\nher. She hinted that she considered his professional reputation was at\r\nstake; it would never do for his own wife to fail in securing at any\r\nrate a pass. This was touching Theobald on a tender spot; he winced and\r\nrejoined with an impatient toss of the head, \xe2\x80\x9cBut, Christina, they\r\n_are_ forgiven you\xe2\x80\x9d; and then he entrenched himself in a firm but\r\ndignified manner behind the Lord\xe2\x80\x99s prayer. When he rose he left the\r\nroom, but called Ernest out to say that he could not wish it prolonged.\r\n\r\nJoey was no more use in quieting his mother\xe2\x80\x99s anxiety than Theobald had\r\nbeen\xe2\x80\x94indeed he was only Theobald and water; at last Ernest, who had not\r\nliked interfering, took the matter in hand, and, sitting beside her,\r\nlet her pour out her grief to him without let or hindrance.\r\n\r\nShe said she knew she had not given up all for Christ\xe2\x80\x99s sake; it was\r\nthis that weighed upon her. She had given up much, and had always tried\r\nto give up more year by year, still she knew very well that she had not\r\nbeen so spiritually minded as she ought to have been. If she had, she\r\nshould probably have been favoured with some direct vision or\r\ncommunication; whereas, though God had vouchsafed such direct and\r\nvisible angelic visits to one of her dear children, yet she had had\r\nnone such herself\xe2\x80\x94nor even had Theobald.\r\n\r\nShe was talking rather to herself than to Ernest as she said these\r\nwords, but they made him open his ears. He wanted to know whether the\r\nangel had appeared to Joey or to Charlotte. He asked his mother, but\r\nshe seemed surprised, as though she expected him to know all about it,\r\nthen, as if she remembered, she checked herself and said, \xe2\x80\x9cAh! yes\xe2\x80\x94you\r\nknow nothing of all this, and perhaps it is as well.\xe2\x80\x9d Ernest could not\r\nof course press the subject, so he never found out which of his near\r\nrelations it was who had had direct communication with an immortal. The\r\nothers never said anything to him about it, though whether this was\r\nbecause they were ashamed, or because they feared he would not believe\r\nthe story and thus increase his own damnation, he could not determine.\r\n\r\nErnest has often thought about this since. He tried to get the facts\r\nout of Susan, who he was sure would know, but Charlotte had been\r\nbeforehand with him. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, Master Ernest,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan, when he began to\r\nquestion her, \xe2\x80\x9cyour ma has sent a message to me by Miss Charlotte as I\r\nam not to say nothing at all about it, and I never will.\xe2\x80\x9d Of course no\r\nfurther questioning was possible. It had more than once occurred to\r\nErnest that Charlotte did not in reality believe more than he did\r\nhimself, and this incident went far to strengthen his surmises, but he\r\nwavered when he remembered how she had misdirected the letter asking\r\nfor the prayers of the congregation. \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself\r\ngloomily, \xe2\x80\x9cshe does believe in it after all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen Christina returned to the subject of her own want of\r\nspiritual-mindedness, she even harped upon the old grievance of her\r\nhaving eaten black puddings\xe2\x80\x94true, she had given them up years ago, but\r\nfor how many years had she not persevered in eating them after she had\r\nhad misgivings about their having been forbidden! Then there was\r\nsomething that weighed on her mind that had taken place before her\r\nmarriage, and she should like\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nErnest interrupted: \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear mother,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are ill and your\r\nmind is unstrung; others can now judge better about you than you can; I\r\nassure you that to me you seem to have been the most devotedly\r\nunselfish wife and mother that ever lived. Even if you have not\r\nliterally given up all for Christ\xe2\x80\x99s sake, you have done so practically\r\nas far as it was in your power, and more than this is not required of\r\nanyone. I believe you will not only be a saint, but a very\r\ndistinguished one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt these words Christina brightened. \xe2\x80\x9cYou give me hope, you give me\r\nhope,\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, and dried her eyes. She made him assure her over and\r\nover again that this was his solemn conviction; she did not care about\r\nbeing a distinguished saint now; she would be quite content to be among\r\nthe meanest who actually got into heaven, provided she could make sure\r\nof escaping that awful Hell. The fear of this evidently was omnipresent\r\nwith her, and in spite of all Ernest could say he did not quite dispel\r\nit. She was rather ungrateful, I must confess, for after more than an\r\nhour\xe2\x80\x99s consolation from Ernest she prayed for him that he might have\r\nevery blessing in this world, inasmuch as she always feared that he was\r\nthe only one of her children whom she should never meet in heaven; but\r\nshe was then wandering, and was hardly aware of his presence; her mind\r\nin fact was reverting to states in which it had been before her\r\nillness.\r\n\r\nOn Sunday Ernest went to church as a matter of course, and noted that\r\nthe ever receding tide of Evangelicalism had ebbed many a stage lower,\r\neven during the few years of his absence. His father used to walk to\r\nthe church through the Rectory garden, and across a small intervening\r\nfield. He had been used to walk in a tall hat, his Master\xe2\x80\x99s gown, and\r\nwearing a pair of Geneva bands. Ernest noticed that the bands were worn\r\nno longer, and lo! greater marvel still, Theobald did not preach in his\r\nMaster\xe2\x80\x99s gown, but in a surplice. The whole character of the service\r\nwas changed; you could not say it was high even now, for high-church\r\nTheobald could never under any circumstances become, but the old\r\neasy-going slovenliness, if I may say so, was gone for ever. The\r\norchestral accompaniments to the hymns had disappeared while my hero\r\nwas yet a boy, but there had been no chanting for some years after the\r\nharmonium had been introduced. While Ernest was at Cambridge, Charlotte\r\nand Christina had prevailed on Theobald to allow the canticles to be\r\nsung; and sung they were to old-fashioned double chants by Lord\r\nMornington and Dr Dupuis and others. Theobald did not like it, but he\r\ndid it, or allowed it to be done.\r\n\r\nThen Christina said: \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear, do you know, I really think\xe2\x80\x9d (Christina\r\nalways \xe2\x80\x9creally\xe2\x80\x9d thought) \xe2\x80\x9cthat the people like the chanting very much,\r\nand that it will be a means of bringing many to church who have stayed\r\naway hitherto. I was talking about it to Mrs Goodhew and to old Miss\r\nWright only yesterday, and they _quite_ agreed with me, but they all\r\nsaid that we ought to chant the \xe2\x80\x98Glory be to the Father\xe2\x80\x99 at the end of\r\neach of the psalms instead of saying it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTheobald looked black\xe2\x80\x94he felt the waters of chanting rising higher and\r\nhigher upon him inch by inch; but he felt also, he knew not why, that\r\nhe had better yield than fight. So he ordered the \xe2\x80\x9cGlory be to the\r\nFather\xe2\x80\x9d to be chanted in future, but he did not like it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally, mamma dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Charlotte, when the battle was won, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\r\nshould not call it the \xe2\x80\x98Glory be to the Father\xe2\x80\x99 you should say\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Gloria.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Christina, and she said \xe2\x80\x9cGloria\xe2\x80\x9d for ever\r\nafter. Then she thought what a wonderfully clever girl Charlotte was,\r\nand how she ought to marry no one lower than a bishop. By-and-by when\r\nTheobald went away for an unusually long holiday one summer, he could\r\nfind no one but a rather high-church clergyman to take his duty. This\r\ngentleman was a man of weight in the neighbourhood, having considerable\r\nprivate means, but without preferment. In the summer he would often\r\nhelp his brother clergymen, and it was through his being willing to\r\ntake the duty at Battersby for a few Sundays that Theobald had been\r\nable to get away for so long. On his return, however, he found that the\r\nwhole psalms were being chanted as well as the Glorias. The influential\r\nclergyman, Christina, and Charlotte took the bull by the horns as soon\r\nas Theobald returned, and laughed it all off; and the clergyman laughed\r\nand bounced, and Christina laughed and coaxed, and Charlotte uttered\r\nunexceptionable sentiments, and the thing was done now, and could not\r\nbe undone, and it was no use grieving over spilt milk; so henceforth\r\nthe psalms were to be chanted, but Theobald grisled over it in his\r\nheart, and he did not like it.\r\n\r\nDuring this same absence what had Mrs Goodhew and old Miss Wright taken\r\nto doing but turning towards the east while repeating the Belief?\r\nTheobald disliked this even worse than chanting. When he said something\r\nabout it in a timid way at dinner after service, Charlotte said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally, papa dear, you _must_ take to calling it the \xe2\x80\x98Creed\xe2\x80\x99 and not\r\nthe \xe2\x80\x98Belief\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d; and Theobald winced impatiently and snorted meek\r\ndefiance, but the spirit of her aunts Jane and Eliza was strong in\r\nCharlotte, and the thing was too small to fight about, and he turned it\r\noff with a laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cAs for Charlotte,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Christina, \xe2\x80\x9cI believe she\r\nknows _everything_.\xe2\x80\x9d So Mrs Goodhew and old Miss Wright continued to\r\nturn to the east during the time the Creed was said, and by-and-by\r\nothers followed their example, and ere long the few who had stood out\r\nyielded and turned eastward too; and then Theobald made as though he\r\nhad thought it all very right and proper from the first, but like it he\r\ndid not. By-and-by Charlotte tried to make him say \xe2\x80\x9cAlleluia\xe2\x80\x9d instead\r\nof \xe2\x80\x9cHallelujah,\xe2\x80\x9d but this was going too far, and Theobald turned, and\r\nshe got frightened and ran away.\r\n\r\nAnd they changed the double chants for single ones, and altered them\r\npsalm by psalm, and in the middle of psalms, just where a cursory\r\nreader would see no reason why they should do so, they changed from\r\nmajor to minor and from minor back to major; and then they got \xe2\x80\x9cHymns\r\nAncient and Modern,\xe2\x80\x9d and, as I have said, they robbed him of his\r\nbeloved bands, and they made him preach in a surplice, and he must have\r\ncelebration of the Holy Communion once a month instead of only five\r\ntimes in the year as heretofore, and he struggled in vain against the\r\nunseen influence which he felt to be working in season and out of\r\nseason against all that he had been accustomed to consider most\r\ndistinctive of his party. Where it was, or what it was, he knew not,\r\nnor exactly what it would do next, but he knew exceedingly well that go\r\nwhere he would it was undermining him; that it was too persistent for\r\nhim; that Christina and Charlotte liked it a great deal better than he\r\ndid, and that it could end in nothing but Rome. Easter decorations\r\nindeed! Christmas decorations\xe2\x80\x94in reason\xe2\x80\x94were proper enough, but Easter\r\ndecorations! well, it might last his time.\r\n\r\nThis was the course things had taken in the Church of England during\r\nthe last forty years. The set has been steadily in one direction. A few\r\nmen who knew what they wanted made cats\xe2\x80\x99 paws of the Christmas and the\r\nCharlottes, and the Christmas and the Charlottes made cats\xe2\x80\x99 paws of the\r\nMrs Goodhews and the old Miss Wrights, and Mrs Goodhews and old Miss\r\nWrights told the Mr Goodhews and young Miss Wrights what they should\r\ndo, and when the Mr Goodhews and the young Miss Wrights did it the\r\nlittle Goodhews and the rest of the spiritual flock did as they did,\r\nand the Theobalds went for nothing; step by step, day by day, year by\r\nyear, parish by parish, diocese by diocese this was how it was done.\r\nAnd yet the Church of England looks with no friendly eyes upon the\r\ntheory of Evolution or Descent with Modification.\r\n\r\nMy hero thought over these things, and remembered many a _ruse_ on the\r\npart of Christina and Charlotte, and many a detail of the struggle\r\nwhich I cannot further interrupt my story to refer to, and he\r\nremembered his father\xe2\x80\x99s favourite retort that it could only end in\r\nRome. When he was a boy he had firmly believed this, but he smiled now\r\nas he thought of another alternative clear enough to himself, but so\r\nhorrible that it had not even occurred to Theobald\xe2\x80\x94I mean the toppling\r\nover of the whole system. At that time he welcomed the hope that the\r\nabsurdities and unrealities of the Church would end in her downfall.\r\nSince then he has come to think very differently, not as believing in\r\nthe cow jumping over the moon more than he used to, or more, probably,\r\nthan nine-tenths of the clergy themselves\xe2\x80\x94who know as well as he does\r\nthat their outward and visible symbols are out of date\xe2\x80\x94but because he\r\nknows the baffling complexity of the problem when it comes to deciding\r\nwhat is actually to be done. Also, now that he has seen them more\r\nclosely, he knows better the nature of those wolves in sheep\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nclothing, who are thirsting for the blood of their victim, and exulting\r\nso clamorously over its anticipated early fall into their clutches. The\r\nspirit behind the Church is true, though her letter\xe2\x80\x94true once\xe2\x80\x94is now\r\ntrue no longer. The spirit behind the High Priests of Science is as\r\nlying as its letter. The Theobalds, who do what they do because it\r\nseems to be the correct thing, but who in their hearts neither like it\r\nnor believe in it, are in reality the least dangerous of all classes to\r\nthe peace and liberties of mankind. The man to fear is he who goes at\r\nthings with the cocksureness of pushing vulgarity and self-conceit.\r\nThese are not vices which can be justly laid to the charge of the\r\nEnglish clergy.\r\n\r\nMany of the farmers came up to Ernest when service was over, and shook\r\nhands with him. He found every one knew of his having come into a\r\nfortune. The fact was that Theobald had immediately told two or three\r\nof the greatest gossips in the village, and the story was not long in\r\nspreading. \xe2\x80\x9cIt simplified matters,\xe2\x80\x9d he had said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9ca good\r\ndeal.\xe2\x80\x9d Ernest was civil to Mrs Goodhew for her husband\xe2\x80\x99s sake, but he\r\ngave Miss Wright the cut direct, for he knew that she was only\r\nCharlotte in disguise.\r\n\r\nA week passed slowly away. Two or three times the family took the\r\nsacrament together round Christina\xe2\x80\x99s death-bed. Theobald\xe2\x80\x99s impatience\r\nbecame more and more transparent daily, but fortunately Christina (who\r\neven if she had been well would have been ready to shut her eyes to it)\r\nbecame weaker and less coherent in mind also, so that she hardly, if at\r\nall, perceived it. After Ernest had been in the house about a week his\r\nmother fell into a comatose state which lasted a couple of days, and in\r\nthe end went away so peacefully that it was like the blending of sea\r\nand sky in mid-ocean upon a soft hazy day when none can say where the\r\nearth ends and the heavens begin. Indeed she died to the realities of\r\nlife with less pain than she had waked from many of its illusions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has been the comfort and mainstay of my life for more than thirty\r\nyears,\xe2\x80\x9d said Theobald as soon as all was over, \xe2\x80\x9cbut one could not wish\r\nit prolonged,\xe2\x80\x9d and he buried his face in his handkerchief to conceal\r\nhis want of emotion.\r\n\r\nErnest came back to town the day after his mother\xe2\x80\x99s death, and returned\r\nto the funeral accompanied by myself. He wanted me to see his father in\r\norder to prevent any possible misapprehension about Miss Pontifex\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nintentions, and I was such an old friend of the family that my presence\r\nat Christina\xe2\x80\x99s funeral would surprise no one. With all her faults I had\r\nalways rather liked Christina. She would have chopped Ernest or any one\r\nelse into little pieces of mincemeat to gratify the slightest wish of\r\nher husband, but she would not have chopped him up for any one else,\r\nand so long as he did not cross her she was very fond of him. By nature\r\nshe was of an even temper, more willing to be pleased than ruffled,\r\nvery ready to do a good-natured action, provided it did not cost her\r\nmuch exertion, nor involve expense to Theobald. Her own little purse\r\ndid not matter; any one might have as much of that as he or she could\r\nget after she had reserved what was absolutely necessary for her dress.\r\nI could not hear of her end as Ernest described it to me without\r\nfeeling very compassionate towards her, indeed her own son could hardly\r\nhave felt more so; I at once, therefore, consented to go down to the\r\nfuneral; perhaps I was also influenced by a desire to see Charlotte and\r\nJoey, in whom I felt interested on hearing what my godson had told me.\r\n\r\nI found Theobald looking remarkably well. Every one said he was bearing\r\nit so beautifully. He did indeed once or twice shake his head and say\r\nthat his wife had been the comfort and mainstay of his life for over\r\nthirty years, but there the matter ended. I stayed over the next day\r\nwhich was Sunday, and took my departure on the following morning after\r\nhaving told Theobald all that his son wished me to tell him. Theobald\r\nasked me to help him with Christina\xe2\x80\x99s epitaph.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would say,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cas little as possible; eulogies of the\r\ndeparted are in most cases both unnecessary and untrue. Christina\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nepitaph shall contain nothing which shall be either the one or the\r\nother. I should give her name, the dates of her birth and death, and of\r\ncourse say she was my wife, and then I think I should wind up with a\r\nsimple text\xe2\x80\x94her favourite one for example, none indeed could be more\r\nappropriate, \xe2\x80\x98Blessed are the pure in heart for they shall see God.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI said I thought this would be very nice, and it was settled. So Ernest\r\nwas sent to give the order to Mr Prosser, the stonemason in the nearest\r\ntown, who said it came from \xe2\x80\x9cthe Beetitudes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER LXXXIV\r\n\r\n\r\nOn our way to town Ernest broached his plans for spending the next year\r\nor two. I wanted him to try and get more into society again, but he\r\nbrushed this aside at once as the very last thing he had a fancy for.\r\nFor society indeed of all sorts, except of course that of a few\r\nintimate friends, he had an unconquerable aversion. \xe2\x80\x9cI always did hate\r\nthose people,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cand they always have hated and always will\r\nhate me. I am an Ishmael by instinct as much as by accident of\r\ncircumstances, but if I keep out of society I shall be less vulnerable\r\nthan Ishmaels generally are. The moment a man goes into society, he\r\nbecomes vulnerable all round.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was very sorry to hear him talk in this way; for whatever strength a\r\nman may have he should surely be able to make more of it if he act in\r\nconcert than alone. I said this.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, \xe2\x80\x9cwhether I make the most of my strength or\r\nnot; I don\xe2\x80\x99t know whether I have any strength, but if I have I dare say\r\nit will find some way of exerting itself. I will live as I like living,\r\nnot as other people would like me to live; thanks to my aunt and you I\r\ncan afford the luxury of a quiet unobtrusive life of self-indulgence,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid he laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cand I mean to have it. You know I like writing,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nadded after a pause of some minutes, \xe2\x80\x9cI have been a scribbler for\r\nyears. If I am to come to the fore at all it must be by writing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI had already long since come to that conclusion myself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, \xe2\x80\x9cthere are a lot of things that want saying which\r\nno one dares to say, a lot of shams which want attacking, and yet no\r\none attacks them. It seems to me that I can say things which not\r\nanother man in England except myself will venture to say, and yet which\r\nare crying to be said.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI said: \xe2\x80\x9cBut who will listen? If you say things which nobody else would\r\ndare to say is not this much the same as saying what everyone except\r\nyourself knows to be better left unsaid just now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I don\xe2\x80\x99t know it; I am bursting with these\r\nthings, and it is my fate to say them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI knew there would be no stopping him, so I gave in and asked what\r\nquestion he felt a special desire to burn his fingers with in the first\r\ninstance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarriage,\xe2\x80\x9d he rejoined promptly, \xe2\x80\x9cand the power of disposing of his\r\nproperty after a man is dead. The question of Christianity is virtually\r\nsettled, or if not settled there is no lack of those engaged in\r\nsettling it. The question of the day now is marriage and the family\r\nsystem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat,\xe2\x80\x9d said I drily, \xe2\x80\x9cis a hornet\xe2\x80\x99s nest indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said he no less drily, \xe2\x80\x9cbut hornet\xe2\x80\x99s nests are exactly what I\r\nhappen to like. Before, however, I begin to stir up this particular one\r\nI propose to travel for a few years, with the especial object of\r\nfinding out what nations now existing are the best, comeliest and most\r\nlovable, and also what nations have been so in times past. I want to\r\nfind out how these people live, and have lived, and what their customs\r\nare.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have very vague notions upon the subject as yet, but the general\r\nimpression I have formed is that, putting ourselves on one side, the\r\nmost vigorous and amiable of known nations are the modern Italians, the\r\nold Greeks and Romans, and the South Sea Islanders. I believe that\r\nthese nice peoples have not as a general rule been purists, but I want\r\nto see those of them who can yet be seen; they are the practical\r\nauthorities on the question\xe2\x80\x94What is best for man? and I should like to\r\nsee them and find out what they do. Let us settle the fact first and\r\nfight about the moral tendencies afterwards.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn fact,\xe2\x80\x9d said I laughingly, \xe2\x80\x9cyou mean to have high old times.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeither higher nor lower,\xe2\x80\x9d was the answer, \xe2\x80\x9cthan those people whom I\r\ncan find to have been the best in all ages. But let us change the\r\nsubject.\xe2\x80\x9d He put his hand into his pocket and brought out a letter. \xe2\x80\x9cMy\r\nfather,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cgave me this letter this morning with the seal\r\nalready broken.\xe2\x80\x9d He passed it over to me, and I found it to be the one\r\nwhich Christina had written before the birth of her last child, and\r\nwhich I have given in an earlier chapter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you do not find this letter,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, \xe2\x80\x9caffect the conclusion which\r\nyou have just told me you have come to concerning your present plans?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe smiled, and answered: \xe2\x80\x9cNo. But if you do what you have sometimes\r\ntalked about and turn the adventures of my unworthy self into a novel,\r\nmind you print this letter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy so?\xe2\x80\x9d said I, feeling as though such a letter as this should have\r\nbeen held sacred from the public gaze.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause my mother would have wished it published; if she had known you\r\nwere writing about me and had this letter in your possession, she would\r\nabove all things have desired that you should publish it. Therefore\r\npublish it if you write at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis is why I have done so.\r\n\r\nWithin a month Ernest carried his intention into effect, and having\r\nmade all the arrangements necessary for his children\xe2\x80\x99s welfare left\r\nEngland before Christmas.\r\n\r\nI heard from him now and again and learnt that he was visiting almost\r\nall parts of the world, but only staying in those places where he found\r\nthe inhabitants unusually good-looking and agreeable. He said he had\r\nfilled an immense quantity of note-books, and I have no doubt he had.\r\nAt last in the spring of 1867 he returned, his luggage stained with the\r\nvariation of each hotel advertisement \xe2\x80\x99twixt here and Japan. He looked\r\nvery brown and strong, and so well favoured that it almost seemed as if\r\nhe must have caught some good looks from the people among whom he had\r\nbeen living. He came back to his old rooms in the Temple, and settled\r\ndown as easily as if he had never been away a day.\r\n\r\nOne of the first things we did was to go and see the children; we took\r\nthe train to Gravesend, and walked thence for a few miles along the\r\nriverside till we came to the solitary house where the good people\r\nlived with whom Ernest had placed them. It was a lovely April morning,\r\nbut with a fresh air blowing from off the sea; the tide was high, and\r\nthe river was alive with shipping coming up with wind and tide.\r\nSea-gulls wheeled around us overhead, sea-weed clung everywhere to the\r\nbanks which the advancing tide had not yet covered, everything was of\r\nthe sea sea-ey, and the fine bracing air which blew over the water made\r\nme feel more hungry than I had done for many a day; I did not see how\r\nchildren could live in a better physical atmosphere than this, and\r\napplauded the selection which Ernest had made on behalf of his\r\nyoungsters.\r\n\r\nWhile we were still a quarter of a mile off we heard shouts and\r\nchildren\xe2\x80\x99s laughter, and could see a lot of boys and girls romping\r\ntogether and running after one another. We could not distinguish our\r\nown two, but when we got near they were soon made out, for the other\r\nchildren were blue-eyed, flaxen-pated little folks, whereas ours were\r\ndark and straight-haired.\r\n\r\nWe had written to say that we were coming, but had desired that nothing\r\nshould be said to the children, so these paid no more attention to us\r\nthan they would have done to any other stranger, who happened to visit\r\na spot so unfrequented except by sea-faring folk, which we plainly were\r\nnot. The interest, however, in us was much quickened when it was\r\ndiscovered that we had got our pockets full of oranges and sweeties, to\r\nan extent greater than it had entered into their small imaginations to\r\nconceive as possible. At first we had great difficulty in making them\r\ncome near us. They were like a lot of wild young colts, very\r\ninquisitive, but very coy and not to be cajoled easily. The children\r\nwere nine in all\xe2\x80\x94five boys and two girls belonging to Mr and Mrs\r\nRollings, and two to Ernest. I never saw a finer lot of children than\r\nthe young Rollings, the boys were hardy, robust, fearless little\r\nfellows with eyes as clear as hawks; the elder girl was exquisitely\r\npretty, but the younger one was a mere baby. I felt as I looked at\r\nthem, that if I had had children of my own I could have wished no\r\nbetter home for them, nor better companions.\r\n\r\nGeorgie and Alice, Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s two children, were evidently quite as one\r\nfamily with the others, and called Mr and Mrs Rollings uncle and aunt.\r\nThey had been so young when they were first brought to the house that\r\nthey had been looked upon in the light of new babies who had been born\r\ninto the family. They knew nothing about Mr and Mrs Rollings being paid\r\nso much a week to look after them. Ernest asked them all what they\r\nwanted to be. They had only one idea; one and all, Georgie among the\r\nrest, wanted to be bargemen. Young ducks could hardly have a more\r\nevident hankering after the water.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what do you want, Alice?\xe2\x80\x9d said Ernest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going to marry Jack here, and be a bargeman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJack was the eldest boy, now nearly twelve, a sturdy little fellow, the\r\nimage of what Mr Rollings must have been at his age. As we looked at\r\nhim, so straight and well grown and well done all round, I could see it\r\nwas in Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s mind as much as in mine that she could hardly do much\r\nbetter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome here, Jack, my boy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ernest, \xe2\x80\x9chere\xe2\x80\x99s a shilling for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThe boy blushed and could hardly be got to come in spite of our\r\nprevious blandishments; he had had pennies given him before, but\r\nshillings never. His father caught him good-naturedly by the ear and\r\nlugged him to us.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a good boy, Jack is,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ernest to Mr Rollings, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure of\r\nthat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr Rollings, \xe2\x80\x9che\xe2\x80\x99s a werry good boy, only that I can\xe2\x80\x99t get\r\nhim to learn his reading and writing. He don\xe2\x80\x99t like going to school,\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s the only complaint I have against him. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nmatter with all my children, and yours, Mr Pontifex, is just as bad,\r\nbut they none of \xe2\x80\x99em likes book learning, though they learn anything\r\nelse fast enough. Why, as for Jack here, he\xe2\x80\x99s almost as good a bargeman\r\nas I am.\xe2\x80\x9d And he looked fondly and patronisingly towards his offspring.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ernest to Mr Rollings, \xe2\x80\x9cif he wants to marry Alice when\r\nhe gets older he had better do so, and he shall have as many barges as\r\nhe likes. In the meantime, Mr Rollings, say in what way money can be of\r\nuse to you, and whatever you can make useful is at your disposal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI need hardly say that Ernest made matters easy for this good couple;\r\none stipulation, however, he insisted on, namely, there was to be no\r\nmore smuggling, and that the young people were to be kept out of this;\r\nfor a little bird had told Ernest that smuggling in a quiet way was one\r\nof the resources of the Rollings family. Mr Rollings was not sorry to\r\nassent to this, and I believe it is now many years since the coastguard\r\npeople have suspected any of the Rollings family as offenders against\r\nthe revenue law.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should I take them from where they are,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ernest to me in the\r\ntrain as we went home, \xe2\x80\x9cto send them to schools where they will not be\r\none half so happy, and where their illegitimacy will very likely be a\r\nworry to them? Georgie wants to be a bargeman, let him begin as one,\r\nthe sooner the better; he may as well begin with this as with anything\r\nelse; then if he shows developments I can be on the look-out to\r\nencourage them and make things easy for him; while if he shows no\r\ndesire to go ahead, what on earth is the good of trying to shove him\r\nforward?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nErnest, I believe, went on with a homily upon education generally, and\r\nupon the way in which young people should go through the embryonic\r\nstages with their money as much as with their limbs, beginning life in\r\na much lower social position than that in which their parents were, and\r\na lot more, which he has since published; but I was getting on in\r\nyears, and the walk and the bracing air had made me sleepy, so ere we\r\nhad got past Greenhithe Station on our return journey I had sunk into a\r\nrefreshing sleep.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER LXXXV\r\n\r\n\r\nErnest being about two and thirty years old and having had his fling\r\nfor the last three or four years, now settled down in London, and began\r\nto write steadily. Up to this time he had given abundant promise, but\r\nhad produced nothing, nor indeed did he come before the public for\r\nanother three or four years yet.\r\n\r\nHe lived as I have said very quietly, seeing hardly anyone but myself,\r\nand the three or four old friends with whom I had been intimate for\r\nyears. Ernest and we formed our little set, and outside of this my\r\ngodson was hardly known at all.\r\n\r\nHis main expense was travelling, which he indulged in at frequent\r\nintervals, but for short times only. Do what he would he could not get\r\nthrough more than about fifteen hundred a year; the rest of his income\r\nhe gave away if he happened to find a case where he thought money would\r\nbe well bestowed, or put by until some opportunity arose of getting rid\r\nof it with advantage.\r\n\r\nI knew he was writing, but we had had so many little differences of\r\nopinion upon this head that by a tacit understanding the subject was\r\nseldom referred to between us, and I did not know that he was actually\r\npublishing till one day he brought me a book and told me flat it was\r\nhis own. I opened it and found it to be a series of semi-theological,\r\nsemi-social essays, purporting to have been written by six or seven\r\ndifferent people, and viewing the same class of subjects from different\r\nstandpoints.\r\n\r\nPeople had not yet forgotten the famous \xe2\x80\x9cEssays and Reviews,\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nErnest had wickedly given a few touches to at least two of the essays\r\nwhich suggested vaguely that they had been written by a bishop. The\r\nessays were all of them in support of the Church of England, and\r\nappeared both by internal suggestion, and their prima facie purport to\r\nbe the work of some half-dozen men of experience and high position who\r\nhad determined to face the difficult questions of the day no less\r\nboldly from within the bosom of the Church than the Church\xe2\x80\x99s enemies\r\nhad faced them from without her pale.\r\n\r\nThere was an essay on the external evidences of the Resurrection;\r\nanother on the marriage laws of the most eminent nations of the world\r\nin times past and present; another was devoted to a consideration of\r\nthe many questions which must be reopened and reconsidered on their\r\nmerits if the teaching of the Church of England were to cease to carry\r\nmoral authority with it; another dealt with the more purely social\r\nsubject of middle class destitution; another with the authenticity or\r\nrather the unauthenticity of the fourth gospel\xe2\x80\x94another was headed\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIrrational Rationalism,\xe2\x80\x9d and there were two or three more.\r\n\r\nThey were all written vigorously and fearlessly as though by people\r\nused to authority; all granted that the Church professed to enjoin\r\nbelief in much which no one could accept who had been accustomed to\r\nweigh evidence; but it was contended that so much valuable truth had\r\ngot so closely mixed up with these mistakes, that the mistakes had\r\nbetter not be meddled with. To lay great stress on these was like\r\ncavilling at the Queen\xe2\x80\x99s right to reign, on the ground that William the\r\nConqueror was illegitimate.\r\n\r\nOne article maintained that though it would be inconvenient to change\r\nthe words of our prayer book and articles, it would not be inconvenient\r\nto change in a quiet way the meanings which we put upon those words.\r\nThis, it was argued, was what was actually done in the case of law;\r\nthis had been the law\xe2\x80\x99s mode of growth and adaptation, and had in all\r\nages been found a righteous and convenient method of effecting change.\r\nIt was suggested that the Church should adopt it.\r\n\r\nIn another essay it was boldly denied that the Church rested upon\r\nreason. It was proved incontestably that its ultimate foundation was\r\nand ought to be faith, there being indeed no other ultimate foundation\r\nthan this for any of man\xe2\x80\x99s beliefs. If so, the writer claimed that the\r\nChurch could not be upset by reason. It was founded, like everything\r\nelse, on initial assumptions, that is to say on faith, and if it was to\r\nbe upset it was to be upset by faith, by the faith of those who in\r\ntheir lives appeared more graceful, more lovable, better bred, in fact,\r\nand better able to overcome difficulties. Any sect which showed its\r\nsuperiority in these respects might carry all before it, but none other\r\nwould make much headway for long together. Christianity was true in so\r\nfar as it had fostered beauty, and it had fostered much beauty. It was\r\nfalse in so far as it fostered ugliness, and it had fostered much\r\nugliness. It was therefore not a little true and not a little false; on\r\nthe whole one might go farther and fare worse; the wisest course would\r\nbe to live with it, and make the best and not the worst of it. The\r\nwriter urged that we become persecutors as a matter of course as soon\r\nas we begin to feel very strongly upon any subject; we ought not\r\ntherefore to do this; we ought not to feel very strongly\xe2\x80\x94even upon that\r\ninstitution which was dearer to the writer than any other\xe2\x80\x94the Church of\r\nEngland. We should be churchmen, but somewhat lukewarm churchmen,\r\ninasmuch as those who care very much about either religion or\r\nirreligion are seldom observed to be very well bred or agreeable\r\npeople. The Church herself should approach as nearly to that of\r\nLaodicea as was compatible with her continuing to be a Church at all,\r\nand each individual member should only be hot in striving to be as\r\nlukewarm as possible.\r\n\r\nThe book rang with the courage alike of conviction and of an entire\r\nabsence of conviction; it appeared to be the work of men who had a\r\nrule-of-thumb way of steering between iconoclasm on the one hand and\r\ncredulity on the other; who cut Gordian knots as a matter of course\r\nwhen it suited their convenience; who shrank from no conclusion in\r\ntheory, nor from any want of logic in practice so long as they were\r\nillogical of malice prepense, and for what they held to be sufficient\r\nreason. The conclusions were conservative, quietistic, comforting. The\r\narguments by which they were reached were taken from the most advanced\r\nwriters of the day. All that these people contended for was granted\r\nthem, but the fruits of victory were for the most part handed over to\r\nthose already in possession.\r\n\r\nPerhaps the passage which attracted most attention in the book was one\r\nfrom the essay on the various marriage systems of the world. It ran:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf people require us to construct,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the writer, \xe2\x80\x9cwe set good\r\nbreeding as the corner-stone of our edifice. We would have it ever\r\npresent consciously or unconsciously in the minds of all as the central\r\nfaith in which they should live and move and have their being, as the\r\ntouchstone of all things whereby they may be known as good or evil\r\naccording as they make for good breeding or against it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat a man should have been bred well and breed others well; that his\r\nfigure, head, hands, feet, voice, manner and clothes should carry\r\nconviction upon this point, so that no one can look at him without\r\nseeing that he has come of good stock and is likely to throw good stock\r\nhimself, this is the _desiderandum_. And the same with a woman. The\r\ngreatest number of these well-bred men and women, and the greatest\r\nhappiness of these well-bred men and women, this is the highest good;\r\ntowards this all government, all social conventions, all art,\r\nliterature and science should directly or indirectly tend. Holy men and\r\nholy women are those who keep this unconsciously in view at all times\r\nwhether of work or pastime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIf Ernest had published this work in his own name I should think it\r\nwould have fallen stillborn from the press, but the form he had chosen\r\nwas calculated at that time to arouse curiosity, and as I have said he\r\nhad wickedly dropped a few hints which the reviewers did not think\r\nanyone would have been impudent enough to do if he were not a bishop,\r\nor at any rate some one in authority. A well-known judge was spoken of\r\nas being another of the writers, and the idea spread ere long that six\r\nor seven of the leading bishops and judges had laid their heads\r\ntogether to produce a volume, which should at once outbid \xe2\x80\x9cEssays and\r\nReviews\xe2\x80\x9d and counteract the influence of that then still famous work.\r\n\r\nReviewers are men of like passions with ourselves, and with them as\r\nwith everyone else _omne ignotum pro magnifico_. The book was really an\r\nable one and abounded with humour, just satire, and good sense. It\r\nstruck a new note and the speculation which for some time was rife\r\nconcerning its authorship made many turn to it who would never have\r\nlooked at it otherwise. One of the most gushing weeklies had a fit over\r\nit, and declared it to be the finest thing that had been done since the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cProvincial Letters\xe2\x80\x9d of Pascal. Once a month or so that weekly always\r\nfound some picture which was the finest that had been done since the\r\nold masters, or some satire that was the finest that had appeared since\r\nSwift or some something which was incomparably the finest that had\r\nappeared since something else. If Ernest had put his name to the book,\r\nand the writer had known that it was by a nobody, he would doubtless\r\nhave written in a very different strain. Reviewers like to think that\r\nfor aught they know they are patting a Duke or even a Prince of the\r\nblood upon the back, and lay it on thick till they find they have been\r\nonly praising Brown, Jones or Robinson. Then they are disappointed, and\r\nas a general rule will pay Brown, Jones or Robinson out.\r\n\r\nErnest was not so much up to the ropes of the literary world as I was,\r\nand I am afraid his head was a little turned when he woke up one\r\nmorning to find himself famous. He was Christina\xe2\x80\x99s son, and perhaps\r\nwould not have been able to do what he had done if he was not capable\r\nof occasional undue elation. Ere long, however, he found out all about\r\nit, and settled quietly down to write a series of books, in which he\r\ninsisted on saying things which no one else would say even if they\r\ncould, or could even if they would.\r\n\r\nHe has got himself a bad literary character. I said to him laughingly\r\none day that he was like the man in the last century of whom it was\r\nsaid that nothing but such a character could keep down such parts.\r\n\r\nHe laughed and said he would rather be like that than like a modern\r\nwriter or two whom he could name, whose parts were so poor that they\r\ncould be kept up by nothing but by such a character.\r\n\r\nI remember soon after one of these books was published I happened to\r\nmeet Mrs Jupp to whom, by the way, Ernest made a small weekly\r\nallowance. It was at Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s chambers, and for some reason we were\r\nleft alone for a few minutes. I said to her: \xe2\x80\x9cMr Pontifex has written\r\nanother book, Mrs Jupp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLor\xe2\x80\x99 now,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, \xe2\x80\x9chas he really? Dear gentleman! Is it about\r\nlove?\xe2\x80\x9d And the old sinner threw up a wicked sheep\xe2\x80\x99s eye glance at me\r\nfrom under her aged eyelids. I forget what there was in my reply which\r\nprovoked it\xe2\x80\x94probably nothing\xe2\x80\x94but she went rattling on at full speed to\r\nthe effect that Bell had given her a ticket for the opera, \xe2\x80\x9cSo, of\r\ncourse,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI went. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t understand one word of it, for it\r\nwas all French, but I saw their legs. Oh dear, oh dear! I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid I\r\nshan\xe2\x80\x99t be here much longer, and when dear Mr Pontifex sees me in my\r\ncoffin he\xe2\x80\x99ll say, \xe2\x80\x98Poor old Jupp, she\xe2\x80\x99ll never talk broad any more\xe2\x80\x99;\r\nbut bless you I\xe2\x80\x99m not so old as all that, and I\xe2\x80\x99m taking lessons in\r\ndancing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this moment Ernest came in and the conversation was changed. Mrs\r\nJupp asked if he was still going on writing more books now that this\r\none was done. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course I am,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m always writing books;\r\nhere is the manuscript of my next;\xe2\x80\x9d and he showed her a heap of paper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell now,\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cdear, dear me, and is that manuscript? I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\noften heard talk about manuscripts, but I never thought I should live\r\nto see some myself. Well! well! So that is really manuscript?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere were a few geraniums in the window and they did not look well.\r\nErnest asked Mrs Jupp if she understood flowers. \xe2\x80\x9cI understand the\r\nlanguage of flowers,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with one of her most bewitching leers,\r\nand on this we sent her off till she should choose to honour us with\r\nanother visit, which she knows she is privileged from time to time to\r\ndo, for Ernest likes her.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER LXXXVI\r\n\r\n\r\nAnd now I must bring my story to a close.\r\n\r\nThe preceding chapter was written soon after the events it records\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nis to say in the spring of 1867. By that time my story had been written\r\nup to this point; but it has been altered here and there from time to\r\ntime occasionally. It is now the autumn of 1882, and if I am to say\r\nmore I should do so quickly, for I am eighty years old and though well\r\nin health cannot conceal from myself that I am no longer young. Ernest\r\nhimself is forty-seven, though he hardly looks it.\r\n\r\nHe is richer than ever, for he has never married and his London and\r\nNorth-Western shares have nearly doubled themselves. Through sheer\r\ninability to spend his income he has been obliged to hoard in\r\nself-defence. He still lives in the Temple in the same rooms I took for\r\nhim when he gave up his shop, for no one has been able to induce him to\r\ntake a house. His house, he says, is wherever there is a good hotel.\r\nWhen he is in town he likes to work and to be quiet. When out of town\r\nhe feels that he has left little behind him that can go wrong, and he\r\nwould not like to be tied to a single locality. \xe2\x80\x9cI know no exception,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe says, \xe2\x80\x9cto the rule that it is cheaper to buy milk than to keep a\r\ncow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs I have mentioned Mrs Jupp, I may as well say here the little that\r\nremains to be said about her. She is a very old woman now, but no one\r\nnow living, as she says triumphantly, can say how old, for the woman in\r\nthe Old Kent Road is dead, and presumably has carried her secret to the\r\ngrave. Old, however, though she is, she lives in the same house, and\r\nfinds it hard work to make the two ends meet, but I do not know that\r\nshe minds this very much, and it has prevented her from getting more to\r\ndrink than would be good for her. It is no use trying to do anything\r\nfor her beyond paying her allowance weekly, and absolutely refusing to\r\nlet her anticipate it. She pawns her flat iron every Saturday for 4d.,\r\nand takes it out every Monday morning for 4.5d. when she gets her\r\nallowance, and has done this for the last ten years as regularly as the\r\nweek comes round. As long as she does not let the flat iron actually go\r\nwe know that she can still worry out her financial problems in her own\r\nhugger-mugger way and had better be left to do so. If the flat iron\r\nwere to go beyond redemption, we should know that it was time to\r\ninterfere. I do not know why, but there is something about her which\r\nalways reminds me of a woman who was as unlike her as one person can be\r\nto another\xe2\x80\x94I mean Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s mother.\r\n\r\nThe last time I had a long gossip with her was about two years ago when\r\nshe came to me instead of to Ernest. She said she had seen a cab drive\r\nup just as she was going to enter the staircase, and had seen Mr\r\nPontifex\xe2\x80\x99s pa put his Beelzebub old head out of the window, so she had\r\ncome on to me, for she hadn\xe2\x80\x99t greased her sides for no curtsey, not for\r\nthe likes of him. She professed to be very much down on her luck. Her\r\nlodgers did use her so dreadful, going away without paying and leaving\r\nnot so much as a stick behind, but to-day she was as pleased as a penny\r\ncarrot. She had had such a lovely dinner\xe2\x80\x94a cushion of ham and green\r\npeas. She had had a good cry over it, but then she was so silly, she\r\nwas.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd there\xe2\x80\x99s that Bell,\xe2\x80\x9d she continued, though I could not detect any\r\nappearance of connection, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s enough to give anyone the hump to see\r\nhim now that he\xe2\x80\x99s taken to chapel-going, and his mother\xe2\x80\x99s prepared to\r\nmeet Jesus and all that to me, and now she ain\xe2\x80\x99t a-going to die, and\r\ndrinks half a bottle of champagne a day, and then Grigg, him as\r\npreaches, you know, asked Bell if I really was too gay, not but what\r\nwhen I was young I\xe2\x80\x99d snap my fingers at any \xe2\x80\x98fly by night\xe2\x80\x99 in Holborn,\r\nand if I was togged out and had my teeth I\xe2\x80\x99d do it now. I lost my poor\r\ndear Watkins, but of course that couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be helped, and then I lost my\r\ndear Rose. Silly faggot to go and ride on a cart and catch the\r\nbronchitics. I never thought when I kissed my dear Rose in Pullen\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nPassage and she gave me the chop, that I should never see her again,\r\nand her gentleman friend was fond of her too, though he was a married\r\nman. I daresay she\xe2\x80\x99s gone to bits by now. If she could rise and see me\r\nwith my bad finger, she would cry, and I should say, \xe2\x80\x98Never mind,\r\nducky, I\xe2\x80\x99m all right.\xe2\x80\x99 Oh! dear, it\xe2\x80\x99s coming on to rain. I do hate a\r\nwet Saturday night\xe2\x80\x94poor women with their nice white stockings and their\r\nliving to get,\xe2\x80\x9d etc., etc.\r\n\r\nAnd yet age does not wither this godless old sinner, as people would\r\nsay it ought to do. Whatever life she has led, it has agreed with her\r\nvery sufficiently. At times she gives us to understand that she is\r\nstill much solicited; at others she takes quite a different tone. She\r\nhas not allowed even Joe King so much as to put his lips to hers this\r\nten years. She would rather have a mutton chop any day. \xe2\x80\x9cBut ah! you\r\nshould have seen me when I was sweet seventeen. I was the very moral of\r\nmy poor dear mother, and she was a pretty woman, though I say it that\r\nshouldn\xe2\x80\x99t. She had such a splendid mouth of teeth. It was a sin to bury\r\nher in her teeth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI only knew of one thing at which she professes to be shocked. It is\r\nthat her son Tom and his wife Topsy are teaching the baby to swear.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! it\xe2\x80\x99s too dreadful awful,\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know the meaning\r\nof the words, but I tell him he\xe2\x80\x99s a drunken sot.\xe2\x80\x9d I believe the old\r\nwoman in reality rather likes it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut surely, Mrs Jupp,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, \xe2\x80\x9cTom\xe2\x80\x99s wife used not to be Topsy. You\r\nused to speak of her as Pheeb.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! yes,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered, \xe2\x80\x9cbut Pheeb behaved bad, and it\xe2\x80\x99s Topsy now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nErnest\xe2\x80\x99s daughter Alice married the boy who had been her playmate more\r\nthan a year ago. Ernest gave them all they said they wanted and a good\r\ndeal more. They have already presented him with a grandson, and I doubt\r\nnot, will do so with many more. Georgie though only twenty-one is owner\r\nof a fine steamer which his father has bought for him. He began when\r\nabout thirteen going with old Rollings and Jack in the barge from\r\nRochester to the upper Thames with bricks; then his father bought him\r\nand Jack barges of their own, and then he bought them both ships, and\r\nthen steamers. I do not exactly know how people make money by having a\r\nsteamer, but he does whatever is usual, and from all I can gather makes\r\nit pay extremely well. He is a good deal like his father in the face,\r\nbut without a spark\xe2\x80\x94so far as I have been able to observe\xe2\x80\x94any literary\r\nability; he has a fair sense of humour and abundance of common sense,\r\nbut his instinct is clearly a practical one. I am not sure that he does\r\nnot put me in mind almost more of what Theobald would have been if he\r\nhad been a sailor, than of Ernest. Ernest used to go down to Battersby\r\nand stay with his father for a few days twice a year until Theobald\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndeath, and the pair continued on excellent terms, in spite of what the\r\nneighbouring clergy call \xe2\x80\x9cthe atrocious books which Mr Ernest Pontifex\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhas written. Perhaps the harmony, or rather absence of discord which\r\nsubsisted between the pair was due to the fact that Theobald had never\r\nlooked into the inside of one of his son\xe2\x80\x99s works, and Ernest, of\r\ncourse, never alluded to them in his father\xe2\x80\x99s presence. The pair, as I\r\nhave said, got on excellently, but it was doubtless as well that\r\nErnest\xe2\x80\x99s visits were short and not too frequent. Once Theobald wanted\r\nErnest to bring his children, but Ernest knew they would not like it,\r\nso this was not done.\r\n\r\nSometimes Theobald came up to town on small business matters and paid a\r\nvisit to Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s chambers; he generally brought with him a couple of\r\nlettuces, or a cabbage, or half-a-dozen turnips done up in a piece of\r\nbrown paper, and told Ernest that he knew fresh vegetables were rather\r\nhard to get in London, and he had brought him some. Ernest had often\r\nexplained to him that the vegetables were of no use to him, and that he\r\nhad rather he would not bring them; but Theobald persisted, I believe\r\nthrough sheer love of doing something which his son did not like, but\r\nwhich was too small to take notice of.\r\n\r\nHe lived until about twelve months ago, when he was found dead in his\r\nbed on the morning after having written the following letter to his\r\nson:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear Ernest,\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ve nothing particular to write about, but your letter\r\nhas been lying for some days in the limbo of unanswered letters, to wit\r\nmy pocket, and it\xe2\x80\x99s time it was answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI keep wonderfully well and am able to walk my five or six miles with\r\ncomfort, but at my age there\xe2\x80\x99s no knowing how long it will last, and\r\ntime flies quickly. I have been busy potting plants all the morning,\r\nbut this afternoon is wet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is this horrid Government going to do with Ireland? I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nexactly wish they\xe2\x80\x99d blow up Mr Gladstone, but if a mad bull would chivy\r\nhim there, and he would never come back any more, I should not be\r\nsorry. Lord Hartington is not exactly the man I should like to set in\r\nhis place, but he would be immeasurably better than Gladstone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI miss your sister Charlotte more than I can express. She kept my\r\nhousehold accounts, and I could pour out to her all little worries, and\r\nnow that Joey is married too, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what I should do if one or\r\nother of them did not come sometimes and take care of me. My only\r\ncomfort is that Charlotte will make her husband happy, and that he is\r\nas nearly worthy of her as a husband can well be.\xe2\x80\x94Believe me, Your\r\naffectionate father,\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTHEOBALD PONTIFEX.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI may say in passing that though Theobald speaks of Charlotte\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmarriage as though it were recent, it had really taken place some six\r\nyears previously, she being then about thirty-eight years old, and her\r\nhusband about seven years younger.\r\n\r\nThere was no doubt that Theobald passed peacefully away during his\r\nsleep. Can a man who died thus be said to have died at all? He has\r\npresented the phenomena of death to other people, but in respect of\r\nhimself he has not only not died, but has not even thought that he was\r\ngoing to die. This is not more than half dying, but then neither was\r\nhis life more than half living. He presented so many of the phenomena\r\nof living that I suppose on the whole it would be less trouble to think\r\nof him as having been alive than as never having been born at all, but\r\nthis is only possible because association does not stick to the strict\r\nletter of its bond.\r\n\r\nThis, however, was not the general verdict concerning him, and the\r\ngeneral verdict is often the truest.\r\n\r\nErnest was overwhelmed with expressions of condolence and respect for\r\nhis father\xe2\x80\x99s memory. \xe2\x80\x9cHe never,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr Martin, the old doctor who\r\nbrought Ernest into the world, \xe2\x80\x9cspoke an ill word against anyone. He\r\nwas not only liked, he was beloved by all who had anything to do with\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA more perfectly just and righteously dealing man,\xe2\x80\x9d said the family\r\nsolicitor, \xe2\x80\x9cI have never had anything to do with\xe2\x80\x94nor one more punctual\r\nin the discharge of every business obligation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall miss him sadly,\xe2\x80\x9d the bishop wrote to Joey in the very warmest\r\nterms. The poor were in consternation. \xe2\x80\x9cThe well\xe2\x80\x99s never missed,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\none old woman, \xe2\x80\x9ctill it\xe2\x80\x99s dry,\xe2\x80\x9d and she only said what everyone else\r\nfelt. Ernest knew that the general regret was unaffected as for a loss\r\nwhich could not be easily repaired. He felt that there were only three\r\npeople in the world who joined insincerely in the tribute of applause,\r\nand these were the very three who could least show their want of\r\nsympathy. I mean Joey, Charlotte, and himself. He felt bitter against\r\nhimself for being of a mind with either Joey or Charlotte upon any\r\nsubject, and thankful that he must conceal his being so as far as\r\npossible, not because of anything his father had done to him\xe2\x80\x94these\r\ngrievances were too old to be remembered now\xe2\x80\x94but because he would never\r\nallow him to feel towards him as he was always trying to feel. As long\r\nas communication was confined to the merest commonplace all went well,\r\nbut if these were departed from ever such a little he invariably felt\r\nthat his father\xe2\x80\x99s instincts showed themselves in immediate opposition\r\nto his own. When he was attacked his father laid whatever stress was\r\npossible on everything which his opponents said. If he met with any\r\ncheck his father was clearly pleased. What the old doctor had said\r\nabout Theobald\xe2\x80\x99s speaking ill of no man was perfectly true as regards\r\nothers than himself, but he knew very well that no one had injured his\r\nreputation in a quiet way, so far as he dared to do, more than his own\r\nfather. This is a very common case and a very natural one. It often\r\nhappens that if the son is right, the father is wrong, and the father\r\nis not going to have this if he can help it.\r\n\r\nIt was very hard, however, to say what was the true root of the\r\nmischief in the present case. It was not Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s having been\r\nimprisoned. Theobald forgot all about that much sooner than nine\r\nfathers out of ten would have done. Partly, no doubt, it was due to\r\nincompatibility of temperament, but I believe the main ground of\r\ncomplaint lay in the fact that he had been so independent and so rich\r\nwhile still very young, and that thus the old gentleman had been robbed\r\nof his power to tease and scratch in the way which he felt he was\r\nentitled to do. The love of teasing in a small way when he felt safe in\r\ndoing so had remained part of his nature from the days when he told his\r\nnurse that he would keep her on purpose to torment her. I suppose it is\r\nso with all of us. At any rate I am sure that most fathers, especially\r\nif they are clergymen, are like Theobald.\r\n\r\nHe did not in reality, I am convinced, like Joey or Charlotte one whit\r\nbetter than he liked Ernest. He did not like anyone or anything, or if\r\nhe liked anyone at all it was his butler, who looked after him when he\r\nwas not well, and took great care of him and believed him to be the\r\nbest and ablest man in the whole world. Whether this faithful and\r\nattached servant continued to think this after Theobald\xe2\x80\x99s will was\r\nopened and it was found what kind of legacy had been left him I know\r\nnot. Of his children, the baby who had died at a day old was the only\r\none whom he held to have treated him quite filially. As for Christina\r\nhe hardly ever pretended to miss her and never mentioned her name; but\r\nthis was taken as a proof that he felt her loss too keenly to be able\r\never to speak of her. It may have been so, but I do not think it.\r\n\r\nTheobald\xe2\x80\x99s effects were sold by auction, and among them the Harmony of\r\nthe Old and New Testaments which he had compiled during many years with\r\nsuch exquisite neatness and a huge collection of MS. sermons\xe2\x80\x94being all\r\nin fact that he had ever written. These and the Harmony fetched\r\nninepence a barrow load. I was surprised to hear that Joey had not\r\ngiven the three or four shillings which would have bought the whole\r\nlot, but Ernest tells me that Joey was far fiercer in his dislike of\r\nhis father than ever he had been himself, and wished to get rid of\r\neverything that reminded him of him.\r\n\r\nIt has already appeared that both Joey and Charlotte are married. Joey\r\nhas a family, but he and Ernest very rarely have any intercourse. Of\r\ncourse, Ernest took nothing under his father\xe2\x80\x99s will; this had long been\r\nunderstood, so that the other two are both well provided for.\r\n\r\nCharlotte is as clever as ever, and sometimes asks Ernest to come and\r\nstay with her and her husband near Dover, I suppose because she knows\r\nthat the invitation will not be agreeable to him. There is a _de haut\r\nen bas_ tone in all her letters; it is rather hard to lay one\xe2\x80\x99s finger\r\nupon it but Ernest never gets a letter from her without feeling that he\r\nis being written to by one who has had direct communication with an\r\nangel. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat an awful creature,\xe2\x80\x9d he once said to me, \xe2\x80\x9cthat angel must\r\nhave been if it had anything to do with making Charlotte what she is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCould you like,\xe2\x80\x9d she wrote to him not long ago, \xe2\x80\x9cthe thoughts of a\r\nlittle sea change here? The top of the cliffs will soon be bright with\r\nheather: the gorse must be out already, and the heather I should think\r\nbegun, to judge by the state of the hill at Ewell, and heather or no\r\nheather\xe2\x80\x94the cliffs are always beautiful, and if you come your room\r\nshall be cosy so that you may have a resting corner to yourself.\r\nNineteen and sixpence is the price of a return-ticket which covers a\r\nmonth. Would you decide just as you would yourself like, only if you\r\ncome we would hope to try and make it bright for you; but you must not\r\nfeel it a burden on your mind if you feel disinclined to come in this\r\ndirection.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I have a bad nightmare,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ernest to me, laughing as he showed\r\nme this letter, \xe2\x80\x9cI dream that I have got to stay with Charlotte.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer letters are supposed to be unusually well written, and I believe it\r\nis said among the family that Charlotte has far more real literary\r\npower than Ernest has. Sometimes we think that she is writing at him as\r\nmuch as to say, \xe2\x80\x9cThere now\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t you think you are the only one of us\r\nwho can write; read this! And if you want a telling bit of descriptive\r\nwriting for your next book, you can make what use of it you like.\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\ndaresay she writes very well, but she has fallen under the dominion of\r\nthe words \xe2\x80\x9chope,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cthink,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cfeel,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9ctry,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cbright,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9clittle,\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\ncan hardly write a page without introducing all these words and some of\r\nthem more than once. All this has the effect of making her style\r\nmonotonous.\r\n\r\nErnest is as fond of music as ever, perhaps more so, and of late years\r\nhas added musical composition to the other irons in his fire. He finds\r\nit still a little difficult, and is in constant trouble through getting\r\ninto the key of C sharp after beginning in the key of C and being\r\nunable to get back again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGetting into the key of C sharp,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cis like an unprotected\r\nfemale travelling on the Metropolitan Railway, and finding herself at\r\nShepherd\xe2\x80\x99s Bush, without quite knowing where she wants to go to. How is\r\nshe ever to get safe back to Clapham Junction? And Clapham Junction\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t quite do either, for Clapham Junction is like the diminished\r\nseventh\xe2\x80\x94susceptible of such enharmonic change, that you can resolve it\r\ninto all the possible termini of music.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTalking of music reminds me of a little passage that took place between\r\nErnest and Miss Skinner, Dr Skinner\xe2\x80\x99s eldest daughter, not so very long\r\nago. Dr Skinner had long left Roughborough, and had become Dean of a\r\nCathedral in one of our Midland counties\xe2\x80\x94a position which exactly\r\nsuited him. Finding himself once in the neighbourhood Ernest called,\r\nfor old acquaintance sake, and was hospitably entertained at lunch.\r\n\r\nThirty years had whitened the Doctor\xe2\x80\x99s bushy eyebrows\xe2\x80\x94his hair they\r\ncould not whiten. I believe that but for that wig he would have been\r\nmade a bishop.\r\n\r\nHis voice and manner were unchanged, and when Ernest remarking upon a\r\nplan of Rome which hung in the hall, spoke inadvertently of the\r\nQuirinal, he replied with all his wonted pomp: \xe2\x80\x9cYes, the QuirInal\xe2\x80\x94or as\r\nI myself prefer to call it, the QuirInal.\xe2\x80\x9d After this triumph he\r\ninhaled a long breath through the corners of his mouth, and flung it\r\nback again into the face of Heaven, as in his finest form during his\r\nhead-mastership.
