At lunch he did indeed once say, \xe2\x80\x9cnext to impossible\r\nto think of anything else,\xe2\x80\x9d but he immediately corrected himself and\r\nsubstituted the words, \xe2\x80\x9cnext to impossible to entertain irrelevant\r\nideas,\xe2\x80\x9d after which he seemed to feel a good deal more comfortable.\r\nErnest saw the familiar volumes of Dr Skinner\xe2\x80\x99s works upon the\r\nbookshelves in the Deanery dining-room, but he saw no copy of \xe2\x80\x9cRome or\r\nthe Bible\xe2\x80\x94Which?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd are you still as fond of music as ever, Mr Pontifex?\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss\r\nSkinner to Ernest during the course of lunch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf some kinds of music, yes, Miss Skinner, but you know I never did\r\nlike modern music.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t that rather dreadful?\xe2\x80\x94Don\xe2\x80\x99t you think you rather\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94she was going\r\nto have added, \xe2\x80\x9cought to?\xe2\x80\x9d but she left it unsaid, feeling doubtless\r\nthat she had sufficiently conveyed her meaning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would like modern music, if I could; I have been trying all my life\r\nto like it, but I succeed less and less the older I grow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd pray, where do you consider modern music to begin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith Sebastian Bach.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd don\xe2\x80\x99t you like Beethoven?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I used to think I did, when I was younger, but I know now that I\r\nnever really liked him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! how can you say so? You cannot understand him, you never could say\r\nthis if you understood him. For me a simple chord of Beethoven is\r\nenough. This is happiness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nErnest was amused at her strong family likeness to her father\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nlikeness which had grown upon her as she had become older, and which\r\nextended even to voice and manner of speaking. He remembered how he had\r\nheard me describe the game of chess I had played with the doctor in\r\ndays gone by, and with his mind\xe2\x80\x99s ear seemed to hear Miss Skinner\r\nsaying, as though it were an epitaph:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStay:\r\nI may presently take\r\nA simple chord of Beethoven,\r\nOr a small semiquaver\r\nFrom one of Mendelssohn\xe2\x80\x99s Songs without Words.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter luncheon when Ernest was left alone for half an hour or so with\r\nthe Dean he plied him so well with compliments that the old gentleman\r\nwas pleased and flattered beyond his wont. He rose and bowed. \xe2\x80\x9cThese\r\nexpressions,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, _voce su\xc3\xa2_, \xe2\x80\x9care very valuable to me.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cThey are\r\nbut a small part, Sir,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Ernest, \xe2\x80\x9cof what anyone of your old\r\npupils must feel towards you,\xe2\x80\x9d and the pair danced as it were a minuet\r\nat the end of the dining-room table in front of the old bay window that\r\nlooked upon the smooth shaven lawn. On this Ernest departed; but a few\r\ndays afterwards, the Doctor wrote him a letter and told him that his\r\ncritics were a \xcf\x83\xce\xba\xce\xbb\xce\xb7\xcf\x81\xce\xbf\xe1\xbd\xb6 \xce\xba\xce\xb1\xe1\xbd\xb6 \xe1\xbc\x80\xce\xbd\xcf\x84\xe1\xbd\xb7\xcf\x84\xcf\x85\xcf\x80\xce\xbf\xce\xb9, and at the same time \xe1\xbc\x80\xce\xbd\xe1\xbd\xb3\xce\xba\xcf\x80\xce\xbb\xce\xb7\xce\xba\xcf\x84\xce\xbf\xce\xb9.\r\nErnest remembered \xcf\x83\xce\xba\xce\xbb\xce\xb7\xcf\x81\xce\xbf\xe1\xbd\xb6, and knew that the other words were something\r\nof like nature, so it was all right. A month or two afterwards, Dr\r\nSkinner was gathered to his fathers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was an old fool, Ernest,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, \xe2\x80\x9cand you should not relent\r\ntowards him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could not help it,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, \xe2\x80\x9che was so old that it was almost\r\nlike playing with a child.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSometimes, like all whose minds are active, Ernest overworks himself,\r\nand then occasionally he has fierce and reproachful encounters with Dr\r\nSkinner or Theobald in his sleep\xe2\x80\x94but beyond this neither of these two\r\nworthies can now molest him further.\r\n\r\nTo myself he has been a son and more than a son; at times I am half\r\nafraid\xe2\x80\x94as for example when I talk to him about his books\xe2\x80\x94that I may\r\nhave been to him more like a father than I ought; if I have, I trust he\r\nhas forgiven me. His books are the only bone of contention between us.\r\nI want him to write like other people, and not to offend so many of his\r\nreaders; he says he can no more change his manner of writing than the\r\ncolour of his hair, and that he must write as he does or not at all.\r\n\r\nWith the public generally he is not a favourite. He is admitted to have\r\ntalent, but it is considered generally to be of a queer unpractical\r\nkind, and no matter how serious he is, he is always accused of being in\r\njest. His first book was a success for reasons which I have already\r\nexplained, but none of his others have been more than creditable\r\nfailures. He is one of those unfortunate men, each one of whose books\r\nis sneered at by literary critics as soon as it comes out, but becomes\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cexcellent reading\xe2\x80\x9d as soon as it has been followed by a later work\r\nwhich may in its turn be condemned.\r\n\r\nHe never asked a reviewer to dinner in his life. I have told him over\r\nand over again that this is madness, and find that this is the only\r\nthing I can say to him which makes him angry with me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat can it matter to me,\xe2\x80\x9d he says, \xe2\x80\x9cwhether people read my books or\r\nnot? It may matter to them\xe2\x80\x94but I have too much money to want more, and\r\nif the books have any stuff in them it will work by-and-by. I do not\r\nknow nor greatly care whether they are good or not. What opinion can\r\nany sane man form about his own work? Some people must write stupid\r\nbooks just as there must be junior ops and third class poll men. Why\r\nshould I complain of being among the mediocrities? If a man is not\r\nabsolutely below mediocrity let him be thankful\xe2\x80\x94besides, the books will\r\nhave to stand by themselves some day, so the sooner they begin the\r\nbetter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI spoke to his publisher about him not long since. \xe2\x80\x9cMr Pontifex,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cis a _homo unius libri_, but it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t do to tell him so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI could see the publisher, who ought to know, had lost all faith in\r\nErnest\xe2\x80\x99s literary position, and looked upon him as a man whose failure\r\nwas all the more hopeless for the fact of his having once made a\r\n_coup_. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is in a very solitary position, Mr Overton,\xe2\x80\x9d continued the\r\npublisher. \xe2\x80\x9cHe has formed no alliances, and has made enemies not only\r\nof the religious world but of the literary and scientific brotherhood\r\nas well. This will not do nowadays. If a man wishes to get on he must\r\nbelong to a set, and Mr Pontifex belongs to no set\xe2\x80\x94not even to a club.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI replied, \xe2\x80\x9cMr Pontifex is the exact likeness of Othello, but with a\r\ndifference\xe2\x80\x94he hates not wisely but too well. He would dislike the\r\nliterary and scientific swells if he were to come to know them and they\r\nhim; there is no natural solidarity between him and them, and if he\r\nwere brought into contact with them his last state would be worse than\r\nhis first. His instinct tells him this, so he keeps clear of them, and\r\nattacks them whenever he thinks they deserve it\xe2\x80\x94in the hope, perhaps,\r\nthat a younger generation will listen to him more willingly than the\r\npresent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan anything,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x99 said the publisher, \xe2\x80\x9cbe conceived more impracticable\r\nand imprudent?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo all this Ernest replies with one word only\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cWait.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuch is my friend\xe2\x80\x99s latest development. He would not, it is true, run\r\nmuch chance at present of trying to found a College of Spiritual\r\nPathology, but I must leave the reader to determine whether there is\r\nnot a strong family likeness between the Ernest of the College of\r\nSpiritual Pathology and the Ernest who will insist on addressing the\r\nnext generation rather than his own. He says he trusts that there is\r\nnot, and takes the sacrament duly once a year as a sop to Nemesis lest\r\nhe should again feel strongly upon any subject. It rather fatigues him,\r\nbut \xe2\x80\x9cno man\xe2\x80\x99s opinions,\xe2\x80\x9d he sometimes says, \xe2\x80\x9ccan be worth holding\r\nunless he knows how to deny them easily and gracefully upon occasion in\r\nthe cause of charity.\xe2\x80\x9d In politics he is a Conservative so far as his\r\nvote and interest are concerned. In all other respects he is an\r\nadvanced Radical. His father and grandfather could probably no more\r\nunderstand his state of mind than they could understand Chinese, but\r\nthose who know him intimately do not know that they wish him greatly\r\ndifferent from what he actually is.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n*** END OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK THE WAY OF ALL FLESH ***\r\n\r\n***** This file should be named 2084-0.txt or 2084-0.zip *****\r\nThis and all associated files of various formats will be found in:\r\n    https://www.gutenberg.org/2/0/8/2084/\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions will\r\nbe renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from print editions not protected by U.S. copyright\r\nlaw means that no one owns a United States copyright in these works,\r\nso the Foundation (and you!) can copy and distribute it in the\r\nUnited States without permission and without paying copyright\r\nroyalties.
